THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal
to the price stated, books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address:
Mining* Books.
There are dozens and scores of mining hand-books, and not one that can be
wholly recommended. As a rule, practical men can’t write, and theorists don’t
know; and the result is that books professedly simple are generally so technical as
to be hopelessly beyond the grasp of the average miner, or even of the instructed
one. Then, again, the treatises extant are mostly published in America and Eng-
land, and deal with American and English conditions. Rudimentary principles, of
course, are everywhere the same; but the Australian application of principles is, for
the most part, totally different in detail. The result is that every miner has to get
his knowledge by the painful old rule-o’-thumb process, falling into precisely the pit-
falls which have snared his predecessors, and winning his way to success, if he ever
reaches it, through difficulties and losses which previous experience should have
saved him from. The undermentioned books are defective from the Austra-
lian point of view, but all of them contain matter which can be turned to valu-
able account by a shrewd head. It often happens that one is puzzled as a prospector
or mine or battery manager for want of a little knowledge of how other people meet
their troubles; and much may be gained from the stored-up wrinkles of others,
however imperfectly set forth.
A PRACTICAL GUIDE TO PROSPECTORS, EXPLORERS, AND MINERS,
by C. W. Moore. Cloth, 15s. 9d. •
This book is especially full and valuable in the matter of blowpipe analysis, and
many colored illustrations are added.
“ELEMENTARY COAL-MINING,” by R. Peel. Cloth, 2s. 9d.
An overseer’s handbook.
“THE PROSPECTOR’S FIELD-BOOK,” by F. S. Osborne. Cloth, Bs. 9d.
Endeavors to solve the prospector’s many riddles in simple language.
“THE HYDRAULIC GOLD-MINER’S MANUAL,” by T. S. G. Kirkpatrick.
Cloth, 6s. 3d. , „
Not at all bad, with many illustrations ; and specially successful because it
confines itself definitely to one'branch of the subject.
“A MANUAL OF MINING,” by M. C. Helsung. Cloth, 21s.
This is a comprehensive American treatise on engines and gear, mine-develop-
ment and working, suitable for managers who want to study up the question
thoroughly.
“THE MINER’S POCKET-BOOK,” by C. G. Lock. Leather, 13s.
“THE MINER’S HANDBOOK,” by John Milne. Cloth, 7s. 9d.
Two very useful books for mine managers and students, with a Wealth of
practical and tabular information. Lock’s is the fuller; but Milne is not to be
despised.
Pretty Prosers.
“ VIRGINIBUS PUERISQUE,” by R. L. Stevenson. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
Essays now well-known, but as fresh as Spring, and perennially charming.
“MY LADY NICOTINE,” by J. M. Barrie, Cloth, 6s. 6d.
A book chief among ten thousand and altogether lovely. The smoker who has
not read it has not smoked.
“ NOUGHTS AND CROSSES,” by A. T. Quiller Couch. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
Surely the quaintest, sweetest stories ever penned, with art reaching its acme
in deliciously subtle simplicity.
“IN A CANADIAN CANOE,” by Barry Pain. Cloth, 4s.
Sketches, verses and stories varying in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
“PROSE FANCIES,” by Richard Le Gallienne. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
A recent book of idyllic essays, throwing the glamour of imagination over the
desert of commonplace, and transmuting lead to .gold by the alchemy of exquisite
prose. “ A Borrowed Sovereign ” should be read at least six times.
“ POST PRANDIAL PHILOSOPHY,” by Grant Allen. Cloth, 4s.
In these articles Grant Allen brings his scientific knowledge and scientific train-
ing to bear effectively on subjects of the day. He is invariably clear and suggestive,
and his tendencies make always for progress.
Contemporary Science.
“ THE EYOLUTION OF SEX,” by Geddes and Thomson. Cloth, 4s.
“ PHYSIOGNOMY AND EXPRESSION,” by P. Mantegazza. Cloth, 4s.
“HYPNOTISM,” by Dr. Albert Moll. Cloth. 4s.
“THE GERM PLASM,” by A. Weismann. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
“THE EVOLUTION OF MODERN CAPITALISM.” Cloth, 4s.
“APPARITIONS AND THOUGHT TRANSFERENCE.” Cloth, 4s.
“THE MAN OF GENIUS,” by Professor Lombroso. Cloth, 4s.
NOTE.—As most of the books mentioned are in strong demand, some may
be temporarily out of stock. In such case, orders will be filed, and the books
forwarded immediately o« arrival of next shipment.
Any book not in this list will be forwarded on receipt of price, particulars of
which will be given to enquirers.

Answers to Correspondents —H.C.: Graham’s “Socialism” (posted, ss. 6d.) is the
best and most impartial book. Going by easy stages you might work up to it with
(pro) Bellamy’s “ Looking Backward ” (Is. 3d.) and [con) Richter’s “ Pictures of the
Socialistic Future ” (Is. 3d.); then [pro) the “Fabian Essays"” (Is. 3d.) and (tow) “ A
Plea for Liberty” (2s. 3d.). Schaffle’s “ Quintessence of Socialism ” (2s. 9d.) is good and
compact J.M.I). : Meiklejohn’s “Book of the English Languase ” (Is. 3d.) is the
handiest introduction to scientific etymology.

Avoiding alike the heat of the Red Sea and

cisco every FOUR WEEKS, as under:—

Honolulu, San Eraneisco, and any points en route
to New York.

MESSAGERIES MARITIMES
SYDNEY to LONDON, via PARIS.
Steamers of 6000 tons, under postal contract with
the French Government, calling at MELBOURNE,
ADELAIDE, ALBANY, MAKE, ADEN, SUEZ, and
PORT SAID, will be despatched monthly as follows:
Passengers booked to BOMBAY, REUNION, MAURI-
TIUS, and EAST COAST OF AFRICA.
Rates of passage to London, FIRST SALOON: £6O to
£7O, including table wines. Liberal concessions to families.
RETURN TICKETS, available for 9 or 12 months, at
REDUCED RATES.—ENGLISH SPOKEN ON BOARD.
Upon arrival at Marseilles, English interpreters meet
the passengers for London and give them every assistance
in landing and passing their lugirage through the Cus-
toms, etc., and also accompany them to Paris and Calais.
N.B.— Passengers' luggage conveyed free of cost to
London by steamer.
For further particulars, apply at the Company’s Office,

Among China’s many difficulties it has
been discovered that a lot of the brass fittings
belonging to the big guns of her fleet have been
pawned or sold to marine stores by various
officers. Before two of the vessels engaged in the
last naval battle could put to sea a whole quantity
of munitions had to be hunted out of various
adjacent three-ball establishments. Even such
things as shells and steam pumps were dis-
covered to be at the local uncle’s. This ought to
takej;he prize in the matter of corruption.
Another quotation from Carey as a reply
to Mark M‘Dermot, who asks, ‘‘What benefit
would a Protective tariff be to the Australian
selector?” By bringing population and his
market near it would enable him to grow turnips,
potatoes, green crops, ‘‘of which the earth pro-
duces tons, instead of wheat which it furnishes in
bushels,” but which from its less bulk can now be
exported to the other end of the world. It would
enable him to convert his grass, now worthless,
into milk, butter and cheese for immediate con-
sumption. It would find him a ready, a steady,
and a larger market without the intervention of
the army of middlemen wiionow prey on him and
the consumer of his produce.

Sheep-station in Maoriland, in 1865.
Highland manager, engaged in distilling whisky
from native cabbage-tree roots. Result, two
bottles of doubtful-looking potheen. This being
the first brew, the brewer, not too sure of the
power of the decoction, puts it by till New Year’s
night, and even then is afraid to sample it.
Two swaggers appear on the scene. A happy
thought strikes Donald ; “I’ll gie them a bottle
o’ my whusky, and see how it affects them."
Carries out his good intentions, and retires. Next
morning makes enquiries, and learns that the
swaggers are alive, and apparently well. “Here
goes then ” —uncorks the remaining bottle —“ if it
will no kill two swaggers, it will no kill me.”
How is that for Scotch caution ?-

Scene, N.S.W. inland town. Cobb and
Co.’s coach ready to start. Heavy swell: “Haw,
groom, have yah got my things packed behind ? ”
Groom: “Yes, sir.” H.S.: “Are you suah?”
Groom: “ Yes, sir.” H.S.: “Now, groom, you
may bring out my rug and handbag. Hurry up,
and don’t keep me waiting.” Groom has a short
and whispered consultation with driver, on seat.
H.S. (to driver): “ What did that man ask you ?”
Driver: “He asked me, sir, who you were, sir,
and I told him, sir, you are the gentleman who
bought Victoria, sir, and that you are just haring
a look at New South Wales, sir.”
When Talmage was in Melbourne he
quoted Edison to the effect that the existence of
an intelligent Creator can almost be proved by
chemistry. By the end of the <-v itury, the intel-
ligent Creator—one almost doubts the intelligence
when contemplating some of His drummers of the
Talmage type—will probably be reduced by chemi-
cal analysis to His constituent elements. The
next step will be to bottle Him up in phials. The
parsons will then turn chemists and retail the
Almighty under a registered brand with the adver-
tised caution to the public:—“Ask for the
Anglican (or Presbyterian, or Wesleyan, or
Calithumpian) Almighty, and see that you get
Him. None other genuine.” We are getting on
famously. The closing years of the last century
were supposed to have produced some pretty
capable blasphemers, but they would be looked
upon as feeble creatures in these days.
Dear Bulletin —Some time ago a par-
ticularly morose “ hatter ” went to a N. Territory
pub. and bought sundry bottles of grog, with
which he departed to his camp near by to have a
solitary orgy. An hour or two later some men
standing on the pub. verandah fancied they smelt
burning flesh, and went over to the camp to
investigate. It was the hatter. He was lying
drunk across a burning log on his face, alive, but
with his chest and stomach roasted to a crisp.
The local trooper —who acts as a court of appeal
in all kinds of bush horrors—was sent for, and
presently arrived with his assistant black tracker.
There was no doctor available, and no magistrate
or other authority. So, when the trooper had
considered the blackened object writhing and
feebly crawling on the grass, he sent his aboriginal
to wet a sack in the river, and with it he humanely
smothered the dreadful apparition, and put it out
of its misery. It was undoubtedly the wisest and
best thing to do under the circumstances, but as j
the matter might have required a lot of explana-
tion before the authorities, the trooper sagaciously
dispensed with an inquest, and instructed his
nigger to bury the dead hatter in the sand. He
was buried accordingly, and there, so far as I
know, the matter dropped. No one knew any-
thing about the deceased; he was one of the
innumerable multitude named Bill whose names
and identity have been lost in the Never Never.
So, if anyone wants to find a missing relative in
these parts, there is a smothered cinder, with a
bag round its head, still waiting to be claimed on
a northern sand-plain,—Yours, &c., H.S.R.
London Times, weekly edition, July 20i
1894, on the evolution of Anti-Semitism :
Our Berlin correspondent tells us that the logical evolu-
tion of Anti-Semitism is progressing with startling
rapidity. First we had Dr. Stocker’s Christian Anti-
Semitism directed against the Jew's w>ho still follow the
Mosaic law. Then came racial Anti-Semitism, which
based its operations on ethnical as distinguished from
religious antipathy. Now we have social Anti-Semitism,
which includes in a common reprobation with Jews by
creed or by descent all capitalists as being Jews by
analogy, and it cannot be denied that this latest form of
Anti-Semitism is rapidly supplanting the older forms,
and appeals much more forcibly to the ear of the masses.
In another direction also the most recent developments
of Anti-Semitism such an orthodox divine
as Dr. Stocker always claims to be food for very
unpalatable reflection. The Bible is, it seems, to be
placed on the Anti-Semitic Index as being permeated
with Jewish falsehoods, and Ahlwardt, in a pamphlet he
has Just published as the outcome of his meditations in
prison, declares that Christ’s genealogy must have been
forged by the Jews, as it is impossible to believe that the
Founder of Christianity or any of His disciples, except
Judas Iscariot, can have sprung from the accursed race.
Yet it is a party fed on such insanities which, in one of
the most intelligent and highly-educated countries in the
world, thrives and prospers more than any other, not so
much amongst the lowest classes as amongst the lower
middle classes of the population, and especially of the
urban population, and, what is even more strange, still
finds countenance and encouragement from the represen-
tatives of extreme Conservatism.
The late Dean of Melbourne used to make
ins sermons esactly half-an-hour long, and, when
preaching, always kept his watch before him so
that his “ Amen ! ’* would fall on the exact tick.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while' to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
A well-known N.S.W. printer, lately
deceased, originally started business by taking
over the printing-ogjoe of an individual who
levanted under peculiar circumstances. He had
got into trouble and devised original methods of
keeping his head above water. He had a peculiar-
looking vehicle with a stylish horse which was
well-known about town; this, the day before he
bolted, he sold to several friends for cash, but
persuaded each buyer to let him have the use of it
for a day or two. Amongst the rest he sold it to a
| priest, and when going away delivered it to his
reverence, who successfully held it against all
claimants. On the Saturday he left he changed
his cheques in several places, pleading that it was
after bank hours. Amongst his largest creditors
was a solicitor to whom he was indebted over
,£5OO. The attorney was on board the steamer
seeing a friend off when he encountered the
printer in travelling-cap and rugs. “Hello, off
for a trip?” jestingly. “Oh, no; come to
see a friend off; ” and in view of the land-
shark the diddler got into a waterman’s boat,
the man of law getting over the other side into
i the steam-tender. The smart printer immediately
reboarded the steamer and got away for ever.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 27, 1894.
The Coming Australian.
Receding tourists, like receding tides, leave
their jetsam upon our shores. Sometimes it
is a mere tangle of weed and scum ; some-
times a shell murmuring lessons learned in
the deep ocean of truth. The flippant im-
pressions of Anthony Trollope and Max
O’Rell, writing chiefly to amuse, are the
merest spume and froth of thought. The
balanced judgment of Froude and the keen
criticism of Francis Adams may be dis-
puted, but must be respected. Of all our
censors, Adams has incomparably the clearest
vision and the surest touch. Miss Shaw is
an admirable chronicler and a worthless
commentator. Her grasp of facts and details
is wonderfully strong, of opinions and prin-
ciples invariably weak. Adams added splen-
did analysis to splendid synthesis ; and even
Marcus Clarke, with as many years spent
in Australia as Adams spent months, has left
us no such body of suggestive thought upon
ourselves and our future as Adams left in
his brilliant Fortnightly essays.
• • »
It is curious and instructive to compare
the impressions of the writers who have
wandered through Australia and sought to
discern the feeble glimmerings of national
tendencies. Trollope, in 1871, thought the
Australian-born laborer superior to the immi-
grant. In other classes, he found the young
native listless, unenergetic, vain, and boast-
ful, coming too quickly to a weak maturity.
“He rarely runs into bad vices. He does
not drink, gamble, or go utterly to the dogs.”
But, if he does nob fall, he cannot rise, and
he is too content in mediocrity. Thirteen
years later came Froude, learned, thought-
ful, severe, aiming at truth ; but always in
leading-strings, fixed in preconceptions, and
seeing only what he was shown. Still, he
saw much, and his intellect enabled him to
sift the real from the merely plausible, the
vital from the unimportant. It is strange,
considering the difference in the two men,
how Froude’s judgment repeats Trollope’s,
whilst enlarging upon it :
Sydney colonists have no severe intellectual interests.
They are courteous and polite . . energetic . . hut
only to conquer the enemies of material comfort, that
their own lives may be bright and pleasant. . . There
were no persons that I met with who showed much
concern about the deeper spiritual problems in the resolu-
tion of which alone man’s life rises into greatness. . .
They have no national concerns and risks. . . They
will have good lawyers among them, good doctors, good
men of science, engineers, merchants, manufacturers, as
the Romans had in the decline of the Empire. But of
the heroic type of man, of whom poets will sing and
after ages be anxious to read, there will not be so many,
when the generation is gone which was bred and horn in
the old world. . . The rising Australians are “promis-
ing young men.” It they mean to be more they must
either be independent or must be citizens of Oceana.
This, though spoken directly of Sydney
residents, has reference to the whole race of
Australians, and particularly to the natives
as Froude saw them. So also has this,
spoken of the Victorians :
With so fair a climate and with life so easy, the Vic-
torians cannot be sad, and it is pleasant to see I people
who know so well how to enjoy themselves. But m< n
and nations require in reserve a certain sternness, and if
anything truly great is ever to come out of them, this
lesson will in time be hammered into them.
The gist of Froude’s criticism is thus that
life is too easy with us, that we lack the
stimulus to be strong, and that our material
prosperity tends to spiritual barrenness.
• * •
Compare Max O’Rell, up to date :
Of all the members of the great Anglo-Saxon family I
think the Australian is destined to become the most easy-
going, the most sociable, and, perhaps, the most cheerful.
. . . You will not find in the Australian that dogged,
obstinate perseverance, that bull-dog tenacity which has
helped the English Io do so many great things, and which
still puts the Scotchman beyond competition in every
enterprise which calls for privations, hard work and in-
domitable tenacity. . . . The Australian has quite a
passion for amusement. There is no country in the world
whose people flock in such numbers to theatres, concerts,
exhibitions, all places of recreation ; there are no people
who take so many holidays, or enter with so much keen-
ness into all national sports ; there is no society that
dines and dances quite so much as Australasian society.
Is there not a striking similarity in the
thought which naturally rises to the lips of
these three surveyors of Australian character,
separated from each other by a decade of
time, yet singularly unanimous in verdict ?
Now take Adams, who brought a more open
mind to utilise greater opportunities of
observation ; though his judgment also was
warped by sickness and fettered by pre-
judice :
The native Australians have too often the self-sufficiency
that is b gotten on self-confidence by ignorance. Lean
and high-strung, with the alternations of languor and
activity which the terrible changefulness of their climate
gives them, they wear themselves out in all they do, mis-
taking the exercise of nervous energy for pleasure. They
have in their underside the taint of cruelly. . . More
and more the characteristics of a careless, pleasure-
loving race are developed as secularly-educated Young
Australia, (he true religious Gallic, gets his way. . . .
The average temper of Australians more and more
shows itself either indifferent or hostile to the outer
world. . . . The heathenism of the bush is intense.
Everyone is at heart a pessimist.
Adams draws a broad line of demarcation
between the residents of the coastal belt and
those of the interior plateau, and looks to
the latter for our best results. “ Frankly,”
he says, “ I find not only all that is genuinely
characteristic in Australia and the Austra-
lians springing from this heart of the laud,
but, also, all that is noblest, kindliest and
best.”
Marcus Clarke, whose famous judgment
of the coming Australian would seem best of
all were the dogma a little less doubtful and
the wit a little less sparkling, divides the
Australia of the future into temperate and
tropical, ruled respectively by a republic and
an aristocracy (being copied in this by Miss
Shaw) :
The Australians will be a fretful, clever, perverse, irrit-
able race . . . selfish, self-reliant, prone to wander,
caring little for home ties . . . freed from the highest
burden of intellectual development (genius). . . . The
Australasian will be a square-headed masterful man, with
full temples, plenty of beard, a keen eye, a stern and yet
sensual mouth. His teeth will be hard, and his lungs
good. He will suffer from liver disease, and become
prematurely bald : average duration of life in the un-
married, fifty-nine; in the married, sixty-nine and a
decimal. ... In another hundred years the average
Australasian will be a tall, coarse, strong-jawed, greedy,
pushing, talented man, excelling in swimming and horse-
manship. His religion will be a form of Presbyterianism;
his national policy a democracy tempered by the rate of
exchange. His wife will be a thin, narrow woman, very
fond of dress and idleness, caring little for her children,
but without sufficient brain-power to sin with zest. In
five hundred years, unless recruited from foreign nations,
the breed will be wholly extinct; but in that five hundred
years it will have changed the face of nature, and swal-
lowed up all our contemporary civilisation.
The differences, as well as the harmonies, of
these critical dicta give scope for endless
debate, with Time as sole qualified arbiter.
But speculation, in spite of uncertainty, will
have always its value. It is in our power by
politic government to alter many of the con-
ditions of national life, and so to weaken, if
we cannot withstand, the currents of de-
terioration in our national character. If
observers and thinkers can but determine
the nature and force of those currents, and
statesmen can erect a barrier to their influ-
ence, the Republic of Australia will long
live to amuse its children with the prophecy,
of Marcus Clarke.
Sydney Spookery.
The Confidence Man lives and thrives in civil-
ised communities, not because he towers
intellectually above his fellows, but because
his fellows, when spurred by a greed responsive
to that which actuates him, are as gullible as
week-old puppies, and, when amused with a
rolling reel the guiding-string of which is
unseen, are as inquisitive as young kittens.
Science, like sociality, has its Confidence Men.
Don goggles, murmur meaningless abracadabras,
cultivate a perpendicular frown on the brow,
vent polysyllables and gibberish, mumble of
marvels, mystify with manipulation, and you
may write your own diploma as a professor, and
the certificate will be unhesitatingly accepted
by the average male or female who yearns to be
thought striding in the van of progress
without taking the trouble to learn any goose-
step. And just as the ordinary Confidence Man
of commerce is not “ the only son of a nobleman
who lias just come into a large fortune and is
travelling around the world doing good by
giving it away in bagfuls to deserving people,”
so the average Confidence Man of science is not
a scientist even of the lowest grade. Both
sets of Confidence Men may be relied upon as
frauds, with the exception that the Confidence
Man of science is sometimes an honest dupe as
well as an unconscious duper, and may be
classed among the hopeless cranks.
# * *
Hypnotism and telepathy have some tendril-
roots buried remotely in animal magnetism,
but their Confidence Men—the showmen who
travel on the game—only by accident ever
sound real emotional chords, and, when they
do so, are more surprised than either the victim
or the spectators. Clairvoyance and prophecy
are in reality the subtle finished reasoning of
the finer processes of ke<jji and unerring intel-
lect ; the Confidence Tricks of clairvoyance,
divining, &c., are the vulgar pretences of the
quack uncouthly factored to match the lethargic
ignorance of the dupes. As the victim of an
ordinary Confidence Man of commerce deserves
no sympathy, mainly because he is betrayed
into stupidity by his own greed and self-
conceit, so the dupes of the Confidence Man of
science merit all their hideous befoolery. Yet.
if nineteenth-century law holds that it is its
duty to protest the simple “mug” from
“ sharps” against whom he cannot protect him-
self, it should also assign to itself the protec-
tion of the smatterer against the Confidence
Man of science. The law has not yet learned
logic. The Confidence Man of commerce
reaches gaol; the Confidence Man of science
founds a sect, or a school of philosophy, and
earns honorable mention from his fellows.
* * •
The human mind is a balloon charged with
volatility capable of infinite expansion. Its
fabric is ordinarily of such poor stuff that its
first, constant, and last necessity is a safety-
valve. Lose the string of the safety-valve, and
burst goes the balloon. The volatility is called
imagination; when the balloon bursts, it is
styled hallucination. The safety-valve is
materialism. The average mind is so cramped,
and the average imagination, when skilfully
heated, is so elastic, that the average man can
no more help rushing after chimeras and exult-
ing in the unrestraint .of impossible miracles
than he can help sneezing when his nostrils are
pungently titillated. Ami because man knows
this of himself and of his fellows, and because
life is chiefly a wild, hard, hungry battle of
grabbing and keeping, the Confidence Men of
science abound in the land.
* * *
Sydney has had an epidemic of “ a contagious
mental disease called Spiritualism.” The im-
mortal sponsor for the Marble Man of Cow
Flat, a medical gentleman of otherwise high
repute, has advertised it with all the
honorary zeal of a poetic, artistic and ardent
temperament. He has essayed researches into
occult forces in “ the strict spirit of scientific
enquiry,” according to the best of his belief
and the worst of his faith. It is true that the
medico declares that he is not a Spiritualist,
and parades a vague theory about sub-conscious
forces, and the self-aggregation of mysterious
invisibilities from living human bodies, and
their formativeness to the sense of vision in a
kindly condition of light—the gloaming, for
choice ; or the subdued glow of muffled gas
lined with red curtains, or something
equally chaste. But the learned gentle-
man deceives himself. In his esoteric heart
he grips the creed of Spiritualism as a feeble
saint who doesn’t hanker much after Paradise,
but only rather fears Sheol, grips at Christian
salvation. And the Confidence Men were not
long in discovering his predilection for the fan-
tastic gospel—which is certainly no gospel in
the true sense of “ glad tidings,” since under it
a man runs the risk of marrying his mother-in-
law for eternity. In the flood-tide of the
doctor’s investigations arrived Annie Besant,
pinnacled on an idolatry of lecturing fame,
armed with a brand - new (to Australians)
religion and a fascinating philosophy, and brim-
ful of yearning to prospect for Truth on Alpine
crests as well as in well-bottoms. With living
spooks of her own, and with weird messages
from dead friends arriving at intervals by tele-
patho-psychical mails, Mrs. Besant was able to
swallow three spiritualistically-raised ghosts at
one draught. And a Sydney paper was found
with greenness and business greed enough to
boom the whole matter seriously.
The pretensions of the Spiritualistic Circle
were of some magnitude. A lady, who seems
to have claimed only the rank of an ama:
teur disciple, while she displayed all the creden-
tials of a professional wonder-worker, was
presented before the public as that rate
Spiritualistic acquisition, a Materialising
Medium. It was alleged that she could not*
only coax the spooks to sit at a table
—the angels in Heaven probably drumstick
tables as accompaniments to harp-drill—to
write messages, to send her into trances (like
another lady at present under the notice of the
Sydney criminal courts), to croon ditties, &c.,
but could actually make them visible in form
until you could photograph them, or feed them
with chocolate-creams. Trumpery tricks, sir h
as “ ringing ” a busted tambourine on a living
arm while the hand was held down (and, in
the deflowered name of science called “ the
passing of matter through matter !”), were not
disdained, but the visible gliding ghott was the
thing, and hundreds and thousands of Syd-
neyites read, wondered—rand believed. And it
was just at this supreme stage, after the
Marble-man-from-Cow-Flat authenticator had
decided to worry a “ Royal ” Society to investi-
gate spookery, that some of the newly-made
believer s had their faith hosed with catastrophe.
A wicked and doubting spectator suddenly
grabbed at the “ spirit-form ” and found that
ho had hold of—the medium ! The, great
materialising priestess ! The trance - laid
medium who was supposed all this time to he
lounging perdu in a cabinet, behind a curtain !
The medium with a black mask on her face and
white muslin on her shoulders
The fact is that human life is full of excep-
tional experiences, for which the human mind
cannot fully account. The Marble Man doctor
can “ will ” a doQr open or shut, without touch-
ing it; but has just enough saving sense to
deny believing'in Spiritualism. Just so. What
has Spiritualism to do with that ? Only just as
much as finding a freckled toad under a cab-
bage leaf has to do with the question of the
binomial theorem. But when the doctor after-
wards asserts that he still believes in the
genuineness of the “materialisations” thusraised
and grabbed, the former prescription should be
enlarged. Somebody should brain bim at once
as a scornful stigma on the Irish.intellect. Let
the reader believe nothing about any spook
that won’t satisfy the' evidence of all the com-
bined senses. Everything else is hallucination,
and hallucination is of the harvest of disease.
In the meantime, let the dead spooks bury their
dead. .

Unfortunately, this schedule is not binding on
all, though many councils will adopt it.
Prahran will stand out, but perhaps not after
its next election. Vic. Public Works Dept,
should, step in and back up Essendon and
the Progressives in this mutter, N.S. Wales
Public Works Dept, having show n the example.
The stock cry against the minimum wage is
that it will shut out the old ami infirm. People
who never gave a penny in decent charity are
suddenly alive to the interests of the old and
inlirm. If these were the only objectors it
might not matter, but men in the ranks of the
workers are often found with the same cry in
their mouths. After all, how many of these
“ infirm ” ones are there ? There are no statis-
tics of the “infirmity” meant in this connec-
tion. “Infirmity” as used by statisticians
means inability to follow any kind of work.
The ages of the people, however, perhaps a (lord
a clue. We have added up, in sections, Hay-
ter’s figures for the Victorian 1891 census, and
give the results here :
Ages 16-19 (males) .. .. .. .. 45,886
~ 20-60 301,009
~ 61-V0 „ .'. „ .. .. 28,959
: „ 71-80 „ .. .. .. ■ 9,080
If we regard the 20-60 men as the able-bodied
of the community, and the 61-70 men as infirm,
we get a proportion of more than 10 sound men
to one unsound. The 16-19 men form a reserve-
fund calculated to more than repair any defi-
ciencies in the 20-60 group. On the other
hand, the 71-80 men may be fairly reckoned to
be out of manual labor altogether. Further,
we wrong the sexagenarians by classing them as
tainted all round. One in four of these old
boys offering for manual labor can his
might on the body of the unbeliever. If, in
addition to this, we reckon the tendency of
old men to withdraw altogether from manual
labor, we shall probably find that not
more than one in 25 who offers for manual
labor,, can be classed as “ old or infirm.”
The cry that goes up against the minimum
wage on behalf of 4 per cent, of the laborers is
therefore either mistaken or hypocritical. It
is charitable to care for the unfortunate, but to
involve a whole community in the misfortunes
of the unfortunate is a crime. Further than
this, the logical minimum wage, such as ap-
proved by Melb. municipal conference, recog-
nises the necessity for a piecework rate, where
practicable, and in stonebreaking the minimum
rate is fixed under which the able and the
partially-disabled can work without injustice.
It will be an easy matter for any municipality
to keep stonebreaking entirely under its own
control, so that the old and infirm shall have a
monopoly of that branch of usefulness. The
old and infirm argument against the minimum-
wage therefore breaks down.

Purves v. O’Hara: A Summing-Up.
Editor Bulletin, —To fairly apportion ihe
blame attributable to each of the combatants in
the recent unseemly fistic encounter between
Dr. O’Hara and Mr. Purves, Q.C., in Collins-
street, Melbourne, is somewhat difficult.'There
seems to have long been bad blood between the
pair, and if the .Q.C. nursed a private grudge
against the medico and misused his honorable
position as counsel by nl I cuing scurrilous com-
ment not sustained by the evidence, he stands
condemned of cowardly conduct. If the
medico, relying on his superior strength and
boxing skill, attacked with hut little warning
an older and weaker man, he also has been
guilty of a cowardly action. Both offenders
have, besides, besmirched themselves by ..their
subsequent indiscreet attempts to excuse’their
conduct. “ Qiri s’excnse,” &c., is only too
applicable here ! From counsel’s statement to
the press interviewer it would seem that personal
feeling was at the bottom of the erratic course
pursued in his speech for the defence in the Db
Albu case. If he were properly instructed in the
matter, as he would lead the public to infer,


unsel signally failed in the duty of cross-
C °amining the witness to the end : it was
e , .q oU sly his own fault that he did not ascer-
+ in from Dr. O’Hara the nature of the opera-
tion in reference to which his subsequent re-
!arks constituted the immediate casus belli.
Having failed, however, to lay a safe founda-
. 0 f indubitable evidence for his attack,
unsel got no more than he deserved from the
fudge's scathing denunciation of the reprehen-
sible license of the summing-up speech. But,
liould not the medico have rested satisfied
with the authoritative judicial rebuke ?
Why, save for notoriety of a very cheap
sort ' pursue the matter further ? It is
true that the medico resorted to his lawyers,
who seem not to have been fully seized
of the facts of the case, or doubtless they would
have advised their client differently. Freedom
of speech on the part of the Bar is but im-
perfectly apprehended by the public. ■ No
counsel should be allowed, nor does the law
Hlow him, to abuse this sacred privilege. On
the contrary, the law expressly forbids it. Nor
can there be a shadow of doubt that counsel in
the case cited could have been cast in heavy
damages for slander. No advocate is licensed
to go beyond his brief and what is brought out
in evidence. If such were not the law, with
human nature as it is, English communities
would not present such an immunity from
personal assaults as they do in connection
with the legal business of the courts. But,
apart from this legal redress, the bragga-
docio of the medico, after the pitiful en-
counter which he had himself commenced, was
a fatal blunder in tactics sufficient to strip him
of all the public sympathy and pecuniary
solatium to which he might have been other-
wise entitled. Doubtless some unsuccessful
litigants will rejoice at the Q.C.’s discomfiture,
and a few weak patients may glory in the
manly prowess of the amateur heavy-weight.
But level-headed men will regard this breach
of the peace with an amused contempt.—
Barrister.
Among the Spirits.
To anybody with an illumined sense of the in-
wardness of outwardness and the inscruta-
bility of the incomprehensible, nothing could
be more satisfactory than Mrs. Mellon’s ex-
planation. Mrs. Mellon is a medium good
enough to exhibit to Sydney society several
properly-guaranteed and well-authenticated
spirits. There are the usual curtained cabinet,
lowered gas, singing in a circle, and so on—the
whole box of tricks, in fact; and then the
spirits come forward and make speeches, eat
chocolate-creams out of the hand, and prove to
Annie Besant (who was “much impressed”)
that Theosophy is all a flam. The other
night, however, a rude sceptic, who had basely
ingratiated himself with the medium by bring-
ing her flowers, made a grab at one of the
spirits and caught the medium —so he says—
with her shoes and stockings off, a mask on her
face, and white muslin round her shoulders.
These things he saw by the light of the gas, which
another wicked sceptic had turned up full. He
also says he saw’ a false beard and other spiri-
tualistic etceteras, but alleges that as soon as
lie let go the medium all the properties vanished
beneath her skirts. Anybody accustomed to
sceptics will see at once that this is the usual
tissue of falsehoods which the bad spirits who
are always with us delight in inventing. For,
hear Mrs. Mellon’s explanation
“I was quite normal up to this time. Mr. Henry [the
sceptic] grabbed the form by the arm. You understand
that part of my being was in this form, and the moment
he made the grab I fell off my chair to my knees. The
people in the room heard me fall, and thought I had
fainted. When I fell I seemed to shoot into the form,
and practically to atsorh if, the consequence being by
this time that Mr. Henry, instead of gripping the form,
had hold of my arm. I should explain that we know
these forms to be made up of atoms and particles from
the atmosphere, and from those in the circle, combined
with the ‘ nerve aura ’ and ‘ life essence ’ of the medium ;
and if anything interferes with the form, which is really
part of the medium, it must necessarily hurt the medium,
and will draw the medium into the form. That fact has
In en demonstrated over and over again, but never in my
own case until this particular instance occurred.
This is as clear as the tailings from a ten-head
battery. The medium and the spirit are quite
different until you grab the spirit, when they
become the same at once. More explanation
“Cissy” [the chocolate-cream spirit] explained that
there was a very inharmonious influence in the room on
Friday evening [when the sceptic grabbed], and that she
Hired what would happen ; but, thinking what might
occur, she and the others of “ the band ” considered that
they had better not entrance me at all on that occasion,
and therefore decided to draw most of the power from the
lower extremities. Hence the attenuation of my lower
hmbs, until they became almost like sticks. The conse-
quence of this was that my shoes and stockings fell off.
1 may say, also, that I was shaking all over.
No one can read this without seeing that
Spiritualism has been once more triumphantly
vindicated, and that scepticism continues to
wallow as usual in the mire of its own unbelief.
Ihe Bulletin accepts the explanation as per-
fectly satisfactory; and only ventures to sug-
gest that in future, in order not to give even a
bare footing to the enemy, all mediums should
wear patent elastic garters.
Concerning' Certain “Vested Interests”
Last week (100 tons of Yankee onions were
in Sydney last week !) a deputation of
New South Wales farmers waited on Premier
Beid and asked for the retention of the present
duties on agricultural produce—a request which
was promptly and emphatically rejected. This
gave Sydney Telegraph an opportunity to
U) ld forth upon the blessings of Foreign-trade
as follows
Vested, interests had sprung up under the tariff.”
I its protective incidence farmers are enabled, it
wnn t 0 a tetter price for their wheat than they
u have to accept, which is only another
him . sa - v ' n « that the rest of the community has to pay a
, “.er pr ee f° r its bread. The vested interest referred
is, therefore, represented by the privilege of putting
f ll ' hands in the pockets of every wage-earner in the
, 0,, .V- No one needs to-be told that this is an interest
J some value, i, u t (,( ie question is—How did it become
Usted 'in the few persons who now lay claim to it?
P | P eo l'le whose pockets have to stand the racket never
neubelately granted it to them.
Ibe weird humor of the situation lies in the
, a °t that bread, flour, and agricultural produce
general have never been so cheap in New
, uth Wales as under the brief Protectionist
regime, and the Telegraph itself has pointed
118 °ut over and over again, and announced it
as 8,11 evidence that Protection is a failure. The
Telegraph is always falling between two
stools. It keeps one writer who demonstrates by
figures that prices have gone down under Protec-
tion, and t hat it is, therefore, a bitter injustice to
the agriculturist. And it keeps another who
asserts vaguely that prices have gone up, and
that the bloated agriculturist is preying on the
community at large. The real state of the case,
as demonstrated by the irresistible evidence of
the market-reports, is that prices have fallen,
but that the agriculturist has benefited by
getting a reliable market instead of, as before,
having a good market for tvvo or three years,
and then being swamped and ruined for
another two or three years by cheap
imports from glutted markets abroad.
Meanwhile the Telegraph’s curse at the
farmer as a Protectionist cormorant who lives
on the vitals of his fellow-men fits in badly
with its former lamentations about the same
farmer as a man who was crushed by an
iniquitous tariff and was longing to throw off
the yoke which had been forged around his
miserable neck.
The Damp Judge.
Quite a number of New South Wales Judges
are reported, as usual, to have been “ moved”
when they made their little official oration over
the late nonagenarian Alfred Stephen, and
one of them, at all events, was “ overcome,”
and had to stop several times in the course of
his speech to wipe away the tear of sensibility
from his eye. The Bar, it is interesting to note,
was likewise moved to a considerable extent,
also as usual. A good many people will pr-o-
bably think it about time to protest against the
theatrical dampness of the legal profession on
such occasions as these. The people who were
so much moved were none of them, so far as
the community is aware, related to the ex-Chief
Justice, and men don’t weep in public, as a
rule, for a casual acquaintance. Moreover, the
legal profession is almost the only one which
weeps publicly for anyone, and as the legal
profession is no more tender-hearted than any
other profession, it is quite impossible to take
its tears in sober earnest. In all seriousness,
The Bulletin finds it hard to believe
that the legal functionaries who were
“ overcome ” at the death of Alfred Stephen
cared very much whether he was dead
or alive, except, perhaps, on the purely selfish
basis that his death reminded them unplea-
santly of their own mortality. And if it had
been Washington instead of Stephen, most of
them would have cared just as little. One
man rarely weeps for another man’s death
unless he occupies such a position that his weep
is sure to get into the papers. The court police-
man, and the gaoler, and the defendant, and
the barrister’s clerk who humps his master’s
papers, being ordinary individuals whose weep
isn’t mentioned in the papers, remain dry, as a
matter of course. If the sobbing was a genuine
emotion it would be no respecter of pe; sons,
and a tender-hearted policeman would be as
liable to break down over the memory of a dead
Judge as the justice on the bench. But he
never does. Weeping is the monopoly of the
bench and the senior bar, and the underling
looks on with a stolid %ye and is
emotionless.
Once Bit, Twice Swindled.
“The time is not now far distant at which it
will become necessary for one or two of the
Victorian banks, which reconstructed last year,
to ask their creditors to assent to a revision of
the terms of schemes of arrangement in one or
two important respects.” Thus the respectable
Argus paves the way for an ignominious crawl
into bankruptcy by the institutions which were
so “ high-standing ” and honorable IS months
ago. There is not a word about the Argus
statements of then and now. Reconstruction,
in most instances, was founded upon bank lies
and newspaper deceit, and, now that the lies
are crumbling and the deceit has shifted its
position, those malodorous receptacles for
cash are about to abase themselves.' The
Argus, an avowed stickler for the just
fulfilment of all obligations, has discovered
that promises to pay depositors within a given
number of years “ could mean nothing but the
liquidation and realisation of a large volume of
assets.” Consequently they were rash pro-
mises, which ought to be cancelled forthwith,
we are gravely told. In short, it was absurd to
pretend that the banks in question were not
hopelessly insolvent, only the pretence was
necessary for the sake of “ public confidence.”
When reconstruction set in, a good slice was
cut off the deposit liability. The banks did not
undertake to pay back to their depositors all that
they had deposited, so, seeing that the realisation
of assets ought to be an everlasting process in
banking business, there should have been a
chance for the scheme to work. Before the
concerns became “temporarily” embarrassed
they were liable to pay money as it fell due.
Less is expected of them now, but the time is
not far distant when they must plead guilty to
having reconstructed under false pretences.
When they officially confess their miserable
condition the time will have come, one imagines,
for taking an inventory of these assets that
can’t be realised for the general reason that
they yield no interest. The real value of the
stuff which is put forward as a guarantee of
full payment in the indefinite future must be
shown to creditors before they can believe it.
A busted butcher has to bare his assets to the
meeting—why shouldn’t a twice-failed bank do
likewise ? A State audit must come. It is
almost here now.
More About Land-Resumption.
What is apparently one of the worst land-
resumption jobs ever perpetrated at the cost of
the people of New South Wales has just come
to light in connection with the Ocean-street
(Sydney) tram-line. When that costly public
work was constructed, about three acres at
Rushcutters’ Bay were wanted for the erection
of engine-houses. Over two acres were obtained
gratis by taking a piece off a public park, and
for the remaining seven-eighths of an acre the
State paid to some person at present unknown
to this paper the sum of £13,000. This was
equal tonearly£ls,oooperacre, or (say) £lOO per
foot frontage. The land in question is situated
a full mile and a-half from Sydney, deep down
in a damp and stinking hollow, beside a huge
drain, in a reclaimed region which was mostly
occupied until recently by Chinamen’s gardens.
The present value of allotments in this un-
wholesome locality is probably not much above
.£l5 a foot; so the Treasury paid for the land
at least six times its fair market value. People
are now asking who sold the land, and what
politician—if any—was he related to ; and at
what price was the land valued for municipal
rating purposes ? Also, what does the
Reid Government propose to do to prevent
any more such iniquities ? It is no earthly
use for the Reid Government to expose the
jobs of the Dibbs regime, and the Dibbs
Government to expose the jobs of the Parkes
regime, and so on ad infinitum, when the
exposure of jobs that are past only helps to
draw public attention from the jobs that are
present. What the country wants is that the
Ministry should hurry up with the Land-Tax
Bill, by which every landowner will be com-
pelled to value his own property for taxation,
and then, when the State wants to resume—
either for Public Works purposes or because it
considers the property to be under-valued —he
will get what he himself declared to be a fair
price, and no more. Until this is done, the
land-resumption business will be a bottomless
pit for the public finances, as before.
Duties and Principles.
A satirical person calling himself “A Vic-
torian Liberal ” wrote, the other day, to Mel-
bourne Age asking information as to how
protectionists can possibly be Liberals in
Australia whilst Liberalism in England favors
Freetrade. The idea of a Freetrade Tory seems
absurd to this British-born dogmatist, inasmuch
as he calls himself a sound Radical, who wants
to abolish high Customs duties. The man who
cannot perceive a difference between manufac-
turing conditions in old Britain and young Vic-
toria, and who will not understand that Victoria
has adopted European and American mearns to
encourage local industries is, generally speak-
ing, an unteachable being. It is not w’orth
anyone’s while to work for his conversion.
Freetrade is his English political religion, and
he clings to that simple faith with the tenacity
of a Bible idolater, who believes that a whale
swallowed Jonah because that unnatural feat
is recorded in Holy Writ. But when “ A Vic-
torian Liberal ” enquires : “ Are we going to
become a prosperous member of a great Aus-
tralian federation or are we going to sink to the
level of the bankrupt States of Southern Europe
and South America ? ” he opens up a new ques-
tion without being aware of its importance.
The impecunious condition of Italy, Greece,
Spain and Portugal, for instance, is no more
chargeable to a protective tariff than the pros-
perity of France is absolutely to be attributed
to the same cause. France, with its huge
standing army, gets richer every year in
defiance of Cobden’s purely British principles.
Its wealth is more equally distributed than the
gold of the little island Freetrade workshop, or
the dollars of the great American protected
Republic, though both of those commercial
rivals have flourished even more than itself, on
paper. Wise land-laws account in a large
measure for the present condition of the French
people. Italy is collapsing under a less
burden of military and naval expenditure, be-
cause it isn’t France, or America, or Great
Britain, or Germany, and for many other
reasons that have nothing whatever to
do with import - duties. Italy is a very
young nation which entered into the
precious Triple Alliance before it had cut its
wisdom teeth. For the rest, bankrupt States
are the result of corrupt Government. To that
“Victorian Liberal” who asks whether his
adopted State is to sink to the financial level
of some other countries, The Bulletin replies
with a significant smile. If the biggest robbers
are always to enjoy Government protection, and
freetrade in fraud is to remain the law
for a privileged class, then Victoria will sink
as aforesaid, without a doubt. Honesty is the
policy that must be established down South
before a demoralised people can make a reaQ
recovery from the effects of their “boom.”
And, since financial roguery has been pretty
closely identified with Freetrade Fat Man, it
follows that purification must come from a
Protectionist Parliament if it is to come at all.

Pitt Town (N.S.W.) co-operative labor settle-
ment (470 souls ; Government subsidy, ,£7OOO ;
cash result of one year’s labor, £5O ; collateral
result, “a beautiful, simple Arcadian exist-
ence ”) sent a deputation to the Minister-in-
charge the other day, and informed that
astounded functionary that 40 per cent, of its
breadwinners are “ incapables ; ” that the dairy
is “ a complete failure ; ” that the bees all flew
away -but one hive ; that they amuse them-
selves cutting firewood (which doesn’t pay), and
“ other people ” make .£5 or .£6 a week carting
it (which does pay); that the Board of
Control buys potatoes at .£3 a ton in
Sydney (paid with Government money)
and retails them on the settlement at
£4 a ton (paid with Government money);
and that they wanted representation on the
Board of Control. Then the Minister up and
added more facts about the “ Wilberforce
Labor Settlement,” which spends 10s. on butter
for every £1 spent on bread ; and the deputa-
tion commiserated the Minister, and the
Minister commiserated the deputation ; and the
interview closed. But the farce continues ;
and the knot of well-meaning persons with
large aspirations and" small intelligences also
continue to frolic round in the guise of little
tin gods dispensing Govt, largesse; and when
the settlement finally expires it will be because
the land was bad, or the management was bad,
or something else was bad ; and in three or
four years new prophets will arise to prove
that the way to settle people on the land is to
take all the shiftless people you cam muster
and spoon-feed them into absolute incapacity
to do a stroke for themselves without Govt.
[i.e, , level-headed and industrious taxpayers )
backing. Then we will start more “co-opera-
tive labor settlements.” Good old human
nature !
The Age, although it clings fondly to the
gallows, advocates a measure of prison-reform,
and favours the theories of Dr. Garth
Wilkinson, who founds his principles on
Swedenborg’s discipline of hell. The idea is
to assess every criminal act at a monetary value,
and set the sum against the name of the
prisoner as a debt he owes to the State, which
must be paid off before he is permitted to go at
large again. This means profitable work for
the prisoners, and competition in the open
market. The notion has points, but it would
work entirely in favour of the smart man. The
best workman would earn the amount of his
debt in perhaps two-thirds of the time that it
would take an inferior tradesman or laborer to
earn a similar amount, and yet, from a moral
point of view, the latter might be the better
man. Because one man can make a dozen pairs
of boots in a given time, and another only nine,
is no reason why the former should serve three
months’ less for burglary or manslaughter. By
the way, will the Age tell us on what principle
the price of deceased would be arrived at in a
case of manslaughter ? Would corporation-
laborers and capitalists be rated on the same
plane, or would there be addilions for Thrift?
When will newspapers abolish the “ respect-
able-looking mam ? ” This intensely original
expression is inspired by the clothes of the
person in the dock, and the accusation doesn’t
concern the clothes but the man himself. The
most villainous bank-embezzler who was ever
brought to justice is “respectable-looking”
because a bank-official must wear clothes of a
certain orthodox pattern ; and the most moral
and law-abiding corporation laborer who w'as
ever wrongly accused is not “respectable-look-
ing,” because the nature of his employment
forbids. So long as this abject reverence for
clothes continues, the only way to secure the
distribution of even-handed justice is to dress
all defendants alike, whether in tweed, or
moleskins, or in evening clothes and a glaring
boiled shirt; or else to undress every defendant
completely and try him “ mid nodings on.” Con-
sidering that the case refers wholly to the man
and not to the clothes, the latter device would
be the more logical, only a fully-clothed jury
would probably give a very poor show to an
undressed defendant. Possibly, if the jury were
also undressed things might be about even.
The story about Lord Rosebery and the
street-walkers who followed him home was, years
ago, told of Gladstone.
One result of Sydney printers’ strike pro-
mises to be the establishment of a co-operative
jobbing office, which will make another cut at the
business profits. Printing is an excellent trade
to which to apply co-operation. The one thing
needful is to get a brainy, honest, energetic
manager, and give him an absolutely free hand to
“sack”loafersand incompetents. Co-operators must
be as hard on themselves as a master would be
hard on them. Once they start a mutual bene-
volent asylum they may put the shutters up.
N. Queensland has experienced the coldest
and most protracted winter felt for the past £0
years. Despite the damage done by over-crop-
ping at Maryborough and Bundaberg, it is esti-
mated that the total production of sugar for this
season in Queensland will exceed 90,000 tons,
valued at well over a million. What with meat-
works going up all along the Northern coast, an
increased production in gold, and the rapid pro-
gress of the sugar-industry, it is fair to assume
that of all the provinces Queensland will be
“ first round the corner.”
The Behring diphtheria-cure, which con-
sists simply in inoculating one diphtheritic animal
with blood-serum from another made immune by
the injection of diphtheria-germs, has reduced the
mortality of patients at Paris children’s hospital
from 60 to 15 per cent. What is this in essence
but a return to the homoeopathic principle almost
universally decried by the medical profession,
while at the same time they applaud vaccination,
based on a similar principle? Here we have
smallpox germs used to cure smallpox, consump-
tion-germs to cure consumption, hydrophobia,
cholera and diphtheria germs set to fight (me
another, and Hahnemann consistently cursed all
the time !
James Anthony Froude, historian, theolo-
gian, biographer, and essayist—a fine example of
a c tntankerous man of letters—is dead at 76. In
an age of faith he would have made a heroic
churchman, and the Zeitgeist which robbed him of
his beliefs soured his temperament and crippled
his judgment. His sphere was literary polemics,
but his heterodoxy nearly always overshot the
mark. His blame is praise, and his praise is fatal.
His literary style is clear, brilliant, and mono-
tonous, wanting life and sympathy. But his
industry and acquirements made him always res-
pected, and much of his work will live long
after him. In his youth he was ordained to the
Church of England, and in mind and body he was
of the true clerical type—hard, fearless and ascetic.
In later life he relinquished his vows, but his vows
never relinquished him. He was a fine militant
figure, and in a fitter environment he would have
been a finer church-militant figure. He fought all
his life, and only leaves the ring at the call of
“ Time!” to fight, if he gets the chance, all through
eternity.
Sargood Softgoods, back again in his old
character of Minister of Defence, is making Vic-
toria as warlike as before. Sargood spells gun-
powder and imperialism and wild alarums. Blood-
thirsty intelligence from Europe sends a thrill
through the department when Sargood occupies
the chair, and the nostrils of the commanding
officers dilate at the prospect of immediate in-
vasions by foreign powers. A few days ago
France was supposed to be calling on Sargood’s
adopted country to draw and defend itself, so the
valiant importer of socks and garters telephoned
to headquarters that there wasn’t another moment
to lose. A couple of Sin. guns were just then
being dismantled at the South Channel Fort (Port
Phillip.) An urgent telegram from Melbourne
ordered these instruments of carnage to be re-
placed in position immediately, and three hours
later there came another furious telegram asking for
information how the work of preparation for the
foe was going on. Next morning a detachment of
artillery leftQueenscliff for the scene of action, and
the guns were fixed up; Sargood, meanwhile,
having carried off the Ministry to inspect the
fleet. It is supposed that a French spy secretly
informed the authorities in Paris of Sargood’s
prompt measures, with the result that the
Republic backed right down. The war-cloud passed
over Sargood’s head, and the panic in Flinders-
lane subsided as rapidly as it had arisen. More-
over, the Czar of Russia, another country that has
dark designs upon Victoria, was taken bad.

The Wild-Cat Column.
When the Melbourne reconstructed banks reduce
their interest on deposits to 3 per cent., what will be the
sulphurous language of those cute individuals who bought
deposit-receipts at 16s. or so? And how will the welkin
ring with their wild cry of “ Where do we come in?” It
is an awful thing to be a cute individual these times.
* * * •
The three worst banks in Australia, according to
public opinion as evidenced by the local share reports, are
the " three O.’s.” There is no market whatever for the
ordinary shares of any one of them. The preference
shares of the City of Melbourne are also unquotable, and
those of the Colonial and Commercial of Australia sell at
about ss. in the £ on their nominal value.
* • * *
A.J.S. Bank shares (£6 15s. paid up), which
sold a tew weeks ago at £2, have been traded off lately at
265., and have now no buyers above 245. Almost all bank
shares have dropped more or less within the past month,
probably by way of another ironical comment on the
“ revival of confidence.” Even the Union, Commercial
of Sydney and New South Wales are moving downward.
• * • «
The Melbourne papers have, within the last
week, suddenly grown loud and brazen and wildly
jnsistant in their demands for a reduction in the rate of
interest payable on reconswindled bank-deposits. The
Age, in particular, calls aloud daily for a reduction from
4J to 3 per cent., and the Argus, which favoured 3£ at
first, is fast coming round to the same view. Neither
paper makes any allusion to the obvious fact that the pro-
posed reduction means a glaring breach of faith with the
depositors, and is partly bankruptcy and partly repudia-
tion. The one point which is kept steadily in view is
that several—probably a good many—of the banks which
were born again must either get a reduction or burst up
for good and all. The situation has long been going
steadily from bad to worse. The ordinary shares of the
Commercial of Australia, City of Melbourne, Colonial, and
even the Victoria, are, to all appearance, unsaleable —
in some oases, the buyer might even get a hand-
some price for taking over the liability ; and the
National is the only one of the Melbourne group whose
ordinary stock is a regularly marketable commodity.
And even its shares sell at over 80 per cent, discount.
Preference shares in the Commercial and Colonial are
offering at a quarter of their paid-up value, and those of
the City of Melb. s°em to have wholly vanished from the
quotations. The last balance-sheets of the Commercial
rndCity show that the profits have dropped almost to
nothing, and with the continuous fall in interest rates it
almost inevitably follows that the next ones will show a
serious loss. Some other balance-sheets, which are about
dne, are expected to pan out in an equally solemn
fashion. The choice between a partial and amicable
repudiation and a i extensive bursting-up sticks out more
conspicuously every day. But what a satire it all is on
the vaunted “restoration of confidence” of last year,
and the fulsome panegyrics which were expended on
Gyles Turner’s great reconstruction device 1
If there is to be another reconstruction, it should
be preceded this time by an independent audit. Two
years ago the directorates of the various fallen institution*
were still reporting regularly the magnificent prosperity
of their respective banks. Eighteen months ago they
reported that the only thing wanted to make (hem per-
manently sound was to refix their deposits at 4J per cent.
Now the same crowd want to meet their creditors, and
announce that a 3 per cent, reconstruction is the one
essential and atier that all will be well. The Bulletin
believes that all the hanks—save, perhaps, one which
seems too far gone for saving —would get through on a
3 p»r cent, basis, under decent management, but no body
of depositors should take any board’s assurance to that
eff ct, or to any other effect, without corroborative evi-
dence. And The Bulletin also believes that only six
reconstructed hanks in all Australia have any reasonable
ohance to survive under the present arrangement.
* » • *
In its issue of 29th September, The Bulletin
drew attention to the following figures showing the
annual income fronj the invested funds of the big life
insurance offices of Australasia, and the amount of interest
and rent outstanding at the date of last balance-sheet:—
Interest and Ditto, Out-
Rents standing and
1893. Accrued.
A.M.P ‘ 668,793 £54,725
Mutual of Victoria .. 44,762 11,925
Mutual of Australasia 52,517 14,046
National Mutual .. 71,047 19,059
Widows’ Fund .. 55,798 11,081
M.L. Government .. 108,097 27,127
Colonial Mutual .. 81,026 55,743
The fact that sticks out conspicuously in the above table
is that the Colonial Mutual has more than twice as large
% proportion of interest and rent outstanding as any other
office. Therefore, the visible inference was that if the
other offices, in such times as the present, could collect
the interest on their mortgages so much better than does
the Colonial, the latter must have the worst and most
dangerous hatch of invest ments of the crowd. The other
possible inference was that, in some cases, instead of
showing overdue interest and rents as a separate item—
which certainly ought to be done in order to enable
policy-holders to estimate the risk of had debts, and to
form an idea of the care with which their money was
Invested—they were disguised by being added on to the
amount under the heading of “ mortgages.”
The Bulletin has since received sufficient
reasonable assurance that the funds of the Colonial
Mutual are carefully invested—generally, in the case of
mortgages, with a 50 per cent, margin—that the actual
losses are very small, and that the accumulation alluded
to arose mainly through the practical impossibility of
collecting interest in the panic year, on many safe invest-
ments, without selling up the mortgagor, hut that the
arrears were, in most casrs, well secured. Also, that the
society’s £530,000 worth or so of bricks and moitar were
mostly acquired" long before the last Australian boom,
and, being scattered all over the world, the depreciation
in some places had been covered to a great extent by a
corresponding rise elsewhere. Assuming, therefore, that
the Colonial Mutual is in a safe and normal condition, it
would be interesting to know how some of the other offices
are situated.
The itpm referred to in the Colonial Mutual’s
sheet reads, “ Interest and rents outstanding and accrued,
£55,743.” The word “ outstanding ” distinctly implies
that it includes all arrears, and the arrears are an impor-
tant item of evidence as to the security of the invest-
ments. The Mutual Life Association, Australian Widows’
Fund, National Mutual and’A.M.P. omit the word “ out-
standing,” therefore there is no evidence that in their
cases the item covers arrears. If it doesn’t cover arrears,
then these must be included in the capital sum of the
mortgages, thus making it impossible to tell the real
position of affairs. Moreover, none of the offices, save
t l e Maoriland Government Department, even professes to
s'-parate accrued interest from arrears, which makes the
information even more scat ty. Taking it all round, the
amount of information which is absent from tho average
balance-sheet is something remarkable.

According to the cable, the Maoriland Govern-
ment has tacked on to its Banking Bill a clause according
to which "the State may guarantee the note-issue of
any bank the assets of which within the colony exceed
the liabilities.” This probably means only any Maoriland
bank—the idea that the Government would guarantee
the note-issue of foreign institutions must be only a cable
aberration—and if the guarantee is accompanied, as it
certainly should be, with large powers in the way of
State inspection and supervision it is a step in the right
-direction. All currency should be as safe as a State
guarantee can make it, and if the Government doesn’t
issue it direct it should at least exercise a cast-iron super-
vision over those who do.

The fact of the co. —after its failure in N.S.W.

weaken its position when it again applies to Parliament
to sanction its huge reach-out. Democratic members can
surely understand that they are asked to hand these

attempt failed to get their measure, had to go into liquid-

deal—leaving the hona-fide purchaser to make his haul
out of a long-suffering and much-fleeced public. The duty
of every Democratic membe* is perfectly clear.

A very large number of the stations in N.S.W.
Eastern Division sheared, or are shearing, under a verbal
agreement (union), while Central and Western Divisions
sheep are handled, mostly, under the P.U. agreement
(non-union). The station properties in the East are little
encumbered, while those out back are in the clutches of
the banks. Membership of the P.U. is therefore by no
means regarded in financial circles as the hall-mark of
miltionairehood.

The far-sighted Sydney speculators who bought
land at the Field of Mars, some seven years ago, on the
understanding that a railway would be built there, are
again agitating for its construction. They seem to think
the present N.S.W. Government a “ good mark.”
That railway should nob be granted to Melb.
Northern suburbs till the Unimproved Value of Land Bill
has been passed. If this Bill provides for a valuation by
every owner of hig land, with State option of purchase at
such value, the way will be prepared for the Northern
Suburbs Railway. The Government will then be able to
pick its route along the cheapest course and act accordin’,
At present the said railway would mean almost the very
worst jobbery on record—unless it be held that the new
Vio. railways are for the benefit of unemployed landlords,

The Victorian Railway Department is seriously
considering the advisability of withdrawing the trains
from a number of hopeless little boom-lines and running
occasional horse-trains in their stead. The spectacle of t
bony steed plodding with a little one-horse train over the
great railway system of Victoria should be the last possi-
bility in the way of a downfall. Unless, indeed, things
grow still worse, and the horse-train has to give place to
an “unemployed” with a barrow at 3s. a day, with a
special bonus when the velocity exceeds 2J knots an hour.
♦ • * ♦
The amalgamation of the Australasian Sugar
Refining Co. and the Colonial Sugar Co. will greatly
benefit the latter. The former, as a Melb. concern,
received Victorian support from the outset and had one
big advantage over its rival by reason of having on its
directorate Melbourne merchants whose travellers pushed
for, and in some cases only booked, orders for the local
company, avoiding small and weak traders and going for
undoubted risks. The Victorian co. easily beat its rival
in Maoriland trade, and its absence from the “ state-
ments ” of many houses that have gone under during the
last two years shows plainly how carefully plum* were
picked for it.

Melbourne Standard, which is just amalga-
mating after losing £40,000, is the seventh paper which
has tried to run against the Evening Herald, and failed.
The World, Tribune, Express, Star, Echo, and Mail
have all gone in dreary succession, while S. V. Winter’s
paper remains still above ground, and S. V. Winter him-
self blasts the errant compositor with all his old-time
vigour and affability.

The reconstructed Vic. Permanent Building
Society got its depositors to agree to a reduction of their
interest from sto 2J per cent, with only one protest. “ I
am 74 years of age,” quavered an old, toothless utterance
out of a long, white, straggly beard from the back of the
hall, “and my deposit is all I have to live upon. What
am Ito do?” But nobody seemed able to suggest any-
thing, and the old depositor put Ids hat on with a
palsied hand and doddered feebly out into the cold, and
the directors assured the meeting that the Vic. Permanent
had still a great future before it if the nr eting would
only pull together. Which is poor consolation to the poor
creditor who will never live to see that great future, and
who hasn’t strength enough left to pull a hair off a pound
of butter.

firm as “Christian gentlemen.” In these suspicious days
very few Anns can stand a knock of that kind.

» » » *
Bendigo has resolved to try if there is anv money
in the small cross reefs and spurs which lie between the
Hustler’s and Garden Gully lines. A large area has been
pegged out, and a cross-cut is to be started. Bendigo
miners have been so busy digging downwards into the
bowels of the globe that the country between its three big
lodes has been left all this time without any systematic
exploration. Possibly the movement may do something
to re-gild the faded glories of Bendigo, but quite as likely
it won’t. The mozzle of the old gold city is out.

Mills’ United (Charters Towers) half-year shows
24,001 tons crushed for 29,2700 z., value £105,933, and
paying £45,000 in divs., with £13,250 in hand. R uindly,
wages for the half-year were £21,000, crushing cost
£17,000, stores £2500, and firewood £l4OO. Total stone
raised since 1889, 135,816 tons, giving 141,6610 z., and
paving £210,000 divs. “ The quartz lode is the largest
and richest in Australia,” say the jubilant directors, and
it goes down like a wall.

* * » *
History repeats itself in that little affair at the
West Volunteer G.M., Lefroy, Tas. The legal manager
and one director of the mine, having heard the lode had
been struck, visited the mine, hut returned to say that
they couldn’t get below' as the feed-pump was damaged
but there was no lode. “On Monday early, shares were
eagerly sought up to 4s. 6d., and the office replied that
there was no information to give. Still the demand kept
up for the shares at a rising rate. On Tuesday the office
telegraphed the mine manager, who replied that he had
struck the lode that day at 230 ft., with splendid gold, and
at 5.30 he wired that the reef was very rich.” It is
always the pump that goes wrong at these critical
moments. Keep your eye on the pump.

One man with a patent gold-saving machine, at
Vio. Mining Show, says he can put a speck of gold through
the eye of a small needle, drop the speck into a ton of
dirt, and then take it out again with his machine.
Another man with another machine says that’s nothing—
he can save the “ eye of the needle ” with his apparatus.

Cabled that the Big Blow at Ooolgardie has
been registered in London with a capital of £150,000.
Registration is not flotation, as many a failure occurs
between. The Big Blow occupies one of the ironstone
ridges two miles out of Ooolgardie on the road to the
Londonderry—and was one of the “blows ” among which
some old pegs were found, indicating that year* before
the field was known some prospectors pegged it. As
they never have been heard of, they were probably starved
to death.

The long-standing feud between the Adelaide
and Melbourne directors of the Central (8.H.) still breaks
out occasionally. Mr. Thomas Walker, ex-Adelaide
director, contributes to the literature on the subject a
letter which alleges that the last div. was paid out of calls
and proceeds of the sale of coke at half cost. He alleges
a loss on the ore still being sold to the Dry Creek Smelt-
ing Co., which, he says, should be avoided by starting
another of the mine durnaees. He also hints that the
Melh. hoard have been intriguing to bring about the re-
signation of Chafley Baker (Adelaide director) and the
mine-manager, who are advocates of home smelting, in
order to revert wholly to the practice of disposing of the
output to the Dry Creek Co.

Gwtdir Examiner lately said that crude kero-
sene-oil had been flowing over the artesian-bore at East
Moree for several days, but this, The Bulletin hears, is
quite incorrect. The foundation for the report appears to
have been that a local mineral lease was taken up to mine
for “coal, shale, and petroleum ” —the latter on the off-
chance, this being the first occasion on which it was
intended to pass right through the coal strata with a drill.

A certain clerk, with a splendid reputation as a
costs-cleik, can so manipulate a “good-day” in the hill
as to make it pass the taxing-master at a guinea at least.
But, alas, since Judge Molesworth cut down a bill of £240
to £3O, although it (the £240) had been through the
taxing-master, one of the most sacred and antiquated
Victorian institutions is in danger.
* * • •
A smart pair lately raised cash at the expense
of an astute time-payment furnisher. A took a house at
a nominal rental, with right of subletting. He sublet to
B, who almost wholly furnished from the time-payment
warehouse. After occupying for a month or so, A
distrains for rent at a boom-rate, and for as long a term
as the law allows. The time-payment people are com-
municated with, pay up, and take their furniture, whilst
the pair go on their way exulting.^
A London softsgoodsman, whose friends paid
£250 apprenticeship money for him to learn his business,
and who was acknowledged to be one of the very best judges
of lace in that city, came out here recently, and the best
chance he could find of employment was the choice
between managing the lace department for a prominent
softgonds house at 30s. a week, and the billet of a nightly
pilgrim at £3 a week. He took the latter, on the ground
that it yielded more money and required less judgment.
* * * *
The discussion among a number of Sydney
bailiffs on a recent evening shaped itself into —Which
naiionality pursues the debtor with most relentlessness ?
The red-nosed men collated many experiences, and, after
throwing out the French, German, Chinese and English
creditor, they settled down hard on the Scotchman, the
Irishman and the Israelite. -Ultimately the Scotchman
was dropped, and on votes being taken the Irishman and
Jew were a tie.

Goodman, the little diamond merchant, who
■was going to turn things upside at Pambula and didn’t,
has settled in Capetown, where he hopes to do a large
’business in Australian products. He showed himself so
plucky a fellow in Australia that hundreds are sincerely
wishing he will get his health back.
