The 40th anniversary of Melb. Age fell on
17th inst. Fat, foul, and forty.
All the members of the staff of Melb.
Herald have got a fortnight’s notice. The co.
that owns the Herald has bought the STANDARD,
and the shareholders of both papers form a new
co. on formal ratification of the arrangement.
The staff of the Herald, when it has swal-
lowed up the Standard, will be composed of the
pickings of both the bid staffs. The Herald is
like one of Pharaoh’s fat kine that gobbled up the
lean beasts. It has swallowed the Tribune,
Echo, World and Evening Mail, and now the
Standard is lost in the.same capacious maw.
Editor Bulletin, —Whilst at Sydney
shipping-office last week the captain of a well-
known clipper asked me if I wanted any seamen,
and proceeded to say he had six men he would
sell me for .£1 each, as he wished to get rid of
them whilst in port. Bat, although this captain
was anxious tp dismiss these men, he would not
pay them compensating wages, but rejoiced that
lie had a cunningly-devised clause in the agree-
ment, by which he could pay them off with Is. a
month. The men’s wishes and rights counted for
nothing. Does not the whole body of navigation
laws need revision ?—Sam Smith, secretary Fede-
rated Seamen’s Union of Australasia.
The printing of the second number of
Cosmos is much improved, but its literary
character remains scrappy and common-place.
‘About Women Politically” is a good and
thoughtful article; Alex. Montgomery supplies a
readable tale; Louise Mack has a new poetic ver-
sion of Heine’s palm and pine-tree ; there is a fine
idea, poorly handled, in “ The Suicide of Sefton ;”
and the blank-verse poets, with lines like —
Vain hope 1 Buddha wept, Jesus prophesied—
are at least amusing. The rest is nothing at all.
But the magazine is unquestionably a big six-
penn’orth.
Fitz Gibbon, of Vic. Metrop. Board of
Works, fulminating against an attempt to reduce
his screw, instanced the sacrifice he had made in
accepting the position of chairman. Amongst
other things he asserted with a glow that he was
debarred from sitting in Parliament. Many
other men are debarred from sitting in Parliament
who don’t get .£2OOO a year as a solace, but the
possibility that he isn’t wanted in the House
would never dawn upon Fitz, who regards himself
as a most stupendous personage. The Gibbon
furthermore complained that Tie was only in
receipt of about half the salary paid to the
fiianagers of important commercial concerns, none
of whom “ had to perform more arduous functions
or maintain more spotless integrity than the
chairman of this Board. ” Of course, in Mel-
bourne, a man should be ’ paid; aocprding to the
extent of the spotless.integrity., be' is palled upon
to maintain.-- If he is-allowed-no?flies on his
integrity, he should certainly be rewarded for the
extra strain.
SUNDRY SHOWS.

Bijou “Ohoufleuri” and Varieties
The Sydney dramatic fbte is over, and
the Melbourne one was nowhere, or even less, by
comparison. The weather probably accounted for
most of the difference; it was a cloudless day and
hot as the hob of Gehenna. Every one had his
straw baton, and his white pants, and his stomach-
girth. The lawn was full, the stand was full, and
the man who was captured late in the day in the
neighborhood of Joe Tolano’s wizard-show, and
borne away by a sympathetic friend, was full also.
This was one of the unintentional spectacles of
the performance, for the inebriated friend was
very large and comatose and the sober friend was
small and fagged out. He continued for half-an-
hour laboriously removing his tight relative, and
by that time got him only half way to the gate.
Then in despair he rolled him into a dark corner,
and lifting his own two horny hands to heaven lie
delivered himself of an apostrophe, like Mark
Antony orating over the drunk body of Csesar.
When he had finished the apostrophe he left. It
was a touching instance of family affection.
* » *
Nellie Stewart, as is her wont, secured the
best position for her tent, and the limelight was
on her all the afternoon, so to speak. The biggest
crowd was round this tent, and most of the men
came out with their hats in their hands as if
Nellie was a kind of a sacrament to be taken in a
devout spirit. Also, you paid about three times
as much for a cup of tea in this tent as in any
other, and then got it capsized down your shirt-
front in the scuffle. But in the way of confiscating
the change Violet Varley and Marietta Nash were
the most enterprising ladies on the field. The
one truly melancholy note of woe which the writer
heard in the whole afternoon was the wail of a
straw-hatted youth who came out with a glazed
look in his eye and told his griefs to a friend out-
side ; “I gave her a sovereign,” he said almost
with tears, “ and she told me she hadn’t any
change.” And then he went away and never
smiled again.
* * #
Miss Virginia Vivienne, in the intervals
between the refreshments, sold flowers on an
improved principle. You paid anything from
Is. upwards—mostly upwards—for the blossom,
and then, finding it was much too large to go into
any human button-hole, the only resource was to
sell it back again to the lovely pirate for 3d., and
leave her to owe you the 3d. She still owes it,
and the coin has gone to the aged and infirm
actors, along with the rest. There was an unpre-
meditated free show at the back of Nellie
Stewart’s tent. The canvas was somewhat brief
just here, and only came down to within a foot of
the grass, and three pairs of ankles were visible at
intervals in consequence. All the afternoon a
small crowd wandered back and forward by this
orifice, and looked hungrily at these ankles, and
asked itself which pair of black stockings was
Nellie’s and which was the property of Florence
Young. One stunted youth lay on his stomach
on the turf for two mortal hours trying to solve
the problem, and came away unsatisfied after all.
At next ffite a totalisator should be set up here,
and the stockings should.be numbered, and then
Young Australia could gamble on these extremi-
ties, less 10 per cent., which would naturally
go to the funds. Round at the back of Kate
Bishop’s hostelry things were differently con-
ducted, and no one could see an ankle here for any
sum.
Nobody was thrilled very much by the
costume football match, or by any of the other
sports save the ladies’ bicycle race, which was
won by Miss Engler in slow time and a neat blue
costume. The crowd mostly walked up and down
and smiled happily, and the occasion was a whole
essay on the teeth of Young and Middle-aged
Australia. The Bulletin doesn’t think much of
the teeth of Middle-aged Australia, anyhow. The
great want of the show was an amateur pawnshop,
with three melons over the door, and Walter
Bentley—who is Scotch—in charge, to advance
money on the watch of the broken johnny who
had spent all his cash on tea and cake with Nellie
Stewart, or who had bought one of Virginia
Vivienne’s ruinous button-holes, or who had
planked down his half-sov. to Violet Varley and
fallen a victim to that lady’s shortness of change.
Most of the tents did a roaring trade, but there
were one or two exceptions. Sterling Whyte
started business as a magician, with Joe Tolano
banging a cymbal outside and addressing the
public in a rich, moist, fruity voice, but the public
didn’t respond, and finally the pair overturned
their country residence in disgust, and went away.
And Fanny Wiseman, in a shabby little tent, like
a hatter’s domicile on a duffer rush, ran a fortune-
telling business, but nobody seemed to care for an
entertainment that had no provisions or drink in
it. So, most of the time, the witch sat there by
herself, a forlorn, neglected little fortune-teller,
and did no visible business. But, wherever there
was anything to eat or to drink, the attendant
angels could only be got at by trampling the
crowd remorselessly underfoot, and when at last
the multitude went home, full of tea and buns and
glory, the takings were -£llOO or thereabouts.
And there are no flies on a fOte which can realise
JjllOO in times like these.
• * •
The committee of the f§te is now
much troubled as to the disposal of the pro-
ceeds. The said proceeds were intended for
the assistance of distressed actors and actresses,
and the question now raised is whether they
should be distributed immediately, or whether
they should be permanently invested and only the
interest applied to charitable purposes. Con-
sidering that the interest would hardly exceed -£4O
a year, and that it would be six months before the
first miserable instalment of .£2O was available,
and further considering that the £2O, when it
came, would hardly be enough to relieve even one
distressed actress or actor, the investment
scheme looks like a foolish mockery. Moreover,
there is very little business capacity about the
profession, and the chances are almost equal that
the fund would be so carefully invested that, in
the courge of a year or two, there would be nothing
but a reconstructed deposit-receipt to show for it.
In any case, where the cash is so small and the
immediate necessities so great, the idea is just
about as brilliant as that of putting out a penny
at 3 per cent, on behalf of a blind beggar, so that
he will have something to fall back upon in his old
age. Consider the joy of being one out of a hun-
dred or more infirm old tragedians with a shadowy
joint claim on the sum of i>2o every six mouths !
It is like forwarding a donation of one prawn to
a starving army.
** * *
During the past week 4 ‘ The Mounte-
banks ” has been stopping a hole to keep the wind
away at Sydney Lyceum, and Nellie Stewart and
May Pollard have been making fitful appearances
and disappearances in the part of Teresa. It is
generally understood that something has been
wrong with thebeloved Nellie’s bronchial tubes or
her oesophagus. Three nights of “ Mikado ” begin
on Saturday, and after that the opera co. packs up
its bag and departs, to make room for a comedy
season run by a fresh crowd just arrived from
London. Said comedy season starts on Wednes-
day with the “ New Boy,” which is reported to be
a piece of extraordinary merit, run by a star
whose merit is also extraordinary.
** * *
“Julius Caesar 1 ' progresses steadily and
profitably in his ancient Roman way at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney). Otherwise thing are very
silent in the theatrical world. The Royal will
be re-opened on Boxing Night by Bland Holt for
a huge season of melodrama, with Misses Hilda
Spong and Virginia Vivienne as the principal
features of loveliness in lieu of Henrietta Watson
and Harrie Ireland departed. Till then the
Royal will probably remain more or less closed.
The Criterion is also empty, and there is no
present sign of it being otherwise. The circus
has gathered up its dogs and monkeys and left,
and except for a few small variety shows and a
picture of one undressed lady, there is nothing
else doing.
** * *
Mr. Arthur Garner’s benefit was an-
nounced to arise like a strong man refreshed, at
Sydney Lyceum, on Wednesday afternoon, with
a fine and varied programme, mostly of a musical
character. The exigencies of a paper which goes
to press on Tuesday night, and is published on
Thursday morning, and dated for Saturday,
make it impossible to say definitely whether the
event really arrived or not, but, unless something
of a wholly unexpected character intervened, it
probably came off in great style and proved a
financial success.

manages the fit’ amateurs who supply the human
element in this spectacular opera, and their
grouping suggests that Johnny’s license to “say
d —n” has been wisely enforced. Also, the
mounting is somewhat magnificent, and the
ballets—one of them by children, and the other by
maidens of marriageable age—are very welcome
revelations. Taken altogether, “Marjorie” is
more than was expected of the Lyric Club. The
name part is prettily spoken and tastefully
sung by Miss Lily Maddooks, a fair young novice,
whose indisposition to assert herself forms a
striking contrast to the sprightly assurance of
Miss Mabelle Davidson, as little Cicely. The
male principals are the saddest things in the
show. Some of them are much more har-
rowing than others, but they all harrow a
good deal. The amorous Norman earl bears
himself with conscious ease and amateur non-
chalance, but he has a fixed expression that leaves
something to be desired. The low comedian,
although mechanically funny at times, would
probably be more at home as Richard the Third.
Then there is a tall young man of the meet-you-
on-the-Blook species, condemned by fate to imper-
sonate Gosric’s cook and jester, but even he is
naught compared with the tenor. The tenor is a
fragile little flower, rendered conspicuous by the
smallness of his head and general want of features
suitable for stage effect. For which matter, the
johnnies of the chorus are not rich in skull-
developrnent. Though many of them suffer
from swollen head you wouldn’t think it to look
at them.

** * *
The Taylor-Carrington crowd, at Melb.
Royal, is growing desperate and changing its
bill twice a week. Last Saturday it was a version
of Fergus Hume’s hansom cab story—that sickest
of successful shilling dreadfuls—produced under
the alias of “Mysteriesof Melbourne.” On Wed-
nesday the co. was to try “ Our Strategists,” a
farcical comedy with an up-country reputation for
extravagant humor. “Rip Van Winkle” is an
awful treat reserved for Saturday next.
** * *
The Bijou show, which improved rapidly
after the first night, is an object of growing
respect. Edwin Kelly, an artist with a stately
presence, took his place beside the beauteous
Miss Leslie Nor man in the nigger part as though he
were the ducal father of that lady come to see that
the tambourine and bones men behaved them-
selves nicely. The Bijou evidently intends to
win a name for doing something different from
the ordinary ’all programme in a superior,
gilt-edged style. Offenbach’s “Ohoufleuri” is
a musical gem, a charming little bit of mock
Italian opera, and the two-shilling dress-circle
begins dimly to understand that Miss Marie
Elster is the best operatic soprano now singing in
Australia. The music, as stated last week, is
capitally rendered in all parts, including a small
part taken by Miss Isabel Stuart. Now that the
singers are warming to their acting business, and
the ballet makes itself appreciated at its proper
worth, the Bijou ought to boom. The Coghills,
with an old and orthodox variety display, have
long been booming at the little theatre downstairs.

to the local morning journals a difference of about £lOOO
a year apiece between their receipts in 1890 and 1894. The
other day there were only two Melbourne theatres adver-
tising—six inches of space altogether.

real Paula Tanqueray.' By the way, in the London

Says a Maoriland paper : “ is as stingy as ever, and

A “duffers’ concert” was recently held for charitable
purposes at Greyiown (M.L.). The one qualification
insisted on was that none of the performers should be
able to sing, and the result was a gathering of all the
saw-like voices for miles round. Any performer who
showed any sign of owning a singing voice was promptly
hooted off the stage as an impostor, while the olhers were
encored and urged to lift up their tuneless roar unto
seventy times seven. Consequently they screamed till far
into the morning.
When Talmage was in Palestine, he “ converted ” an
elderly Yankee millionaire belonging to the party and
baptised him in the Jordan. Talmage and the convert
were both dressed in nothing save a pair of blue bathing-
pants apiece, and it was such a sight as hadn’t been seen
in Palestine since John appeared in his camel’s-hair
costume long ago.

What is fame? A Melb. paper devoted a column to a
drivelling narrative of how Mias Harding was nearly hurt
in a carriage-accident. The interest of the story was
supposed to lie in Miss Harding’s personal celebrity. The
reporter and the editor knew so little of the lady that she
figured in the report as the “premiere danseuse” in
“Morocco Bound.”
On the last night of Inigo Tyrell’s season at Melb.
Alexandra, some degraded ruffian stole Augustus Glover’s
street pants out of his dressing-room. There wasn't one
pair of pants in the co. which would anything like fit the
vast Augustus, and he objected to go home in brigand
attire. At last the next-biggest man in the co. produced
a garment which came down a little way below the
victim’s knees, and Augustus started for home in it amid
great popular excitement, and ran for his life the whole
way.
Harwood celebrated his 64th birthday last Saturday.
Brough and Boucicault boast that they never take any
stock in press notices. When Thespians, therefore, go to
them for a billet, the scrap-book is shoo’d away, and the
applicants are invited instead to give a “ taste of their
quality ” in a recitation or reading from some selected
play.
Elton and J. L. Shine are jealous of each other in a pre-
tentiously affable sort of fashion ; and both together look
askance at young Stayton, who has managed to make his
little part in “ Morocco Bound ’’ funnier than that of
either of the principals. With two “star” comedians in
one co., it ought to be dangerous for anyone else to be a
bit funnier than an undertaker in his business mood, or a
Presbyterian elder sorting the threepenny-bits from the
trouser-buttons in the collection-bag.

Miss Miller, who has been to England to study and is

Thus a Yankee scribe; “On the back of his neck are
two lovely rolls of flesh, with a crease between them. It
was in this crease that the sword of the Queen struck
him when he kneeled to be knighted, and he does it up
in plaster-of-paris at night so that it will not lose its
shape.”

Personal Items.
Justice Boucaut, of S.A., will be 63 on
Monday, Oct. 29.
Graham Berry says he will not wear a wig
as Speaker of Vic. Assembly. Graham is going
in baldheaded.
Dr. O’Hara, of Melbourne, had a lame
hand after “ plugging ” Purves. It is bad policy
to land any lawyer on the jaw.
Recent events almost made it opportune
to declare Tom Butler, of Sydney Freeman’s
Journal, a Knight of St. Gregory.
The late Dr. Oliver Wendell Holmes and
Baronet Clarke (Vic.) were posted honorary
LL.D’s. at Cambridge on the same list.
Why is Rev. Geo. Walters, Unitarian
pastor of Sydney and Melbourne, so persistently
described in the press as a “Christian” clergy-
man ?
Purves, Q.C., got in with his left an art-
ful blow upon O’Hara. But not during the street
fight. Afterwards, in conversation with a news-
paper reporter.

who lately retired after more than 40 years’ service
with .£4OOO compensation, has started private
practice in Adelaide,
Attorney Bleechmore, business partner of
the long-headed S.A. Crown Solicitor, Stuart, has
been christened by sarcastic Villeneuve Smith,

Earl Grey, who has just died, aged 92,
was the Minister controlling the colonies in Lord
Melbourne’s administration, when Victoria and
South Australia were founded.
A new R.C. magazine, Australasian in
character, will appear, under episcopal direction,
about the end of the year; and Card. Moran will
probably lead off the literature.

Albury, and J. B. Tickle, of Sydney, have been
proved at £424,206, and <£39,665 ; but in neither
case is a single copper left to charity.

on the rich dead, hopes to gain from <£25,000 to
<£30,000 by the death of John Dunn, of Mount
Barker, who was reputedly worth <£250,000.

Th eologically and scientifically, Father Curran is
taking to the footsteps of the late Tenison-Woods.
Since his accession to office, N.S.W.
Premier Reid has plainly let the daily newspapers
see that they don’t own his soul. They are fairly
astonished, as they had hoped to absolutely run the
country.
Ex-Chief-Justice Stephen did not long
survive the release of Bertrand, which he had ever
strongly opposed. And he died, curiously enough,
at nine o’clock on Monday morning, the time
usually fixed for executions.
It was in a room full of bookmakers at the
Victoria Hotel, Geelong, after the Geelong races
(says To-Day), and Mr. Andrew Chirnside was
boasting. The bookmakers stood it for some time
and then Barney Thompson said to him : “ What’s
the good of your blowing about your money? A
log could have made it when you did.”
Few people are aware that the
great Melbourne drapery business of
Buckley and Nunn (sold to a syndicate
some time back) owed much to the
shrewdness of the late Farmer, founder
of the well-known Sydney soft-goods
house. In 1848, when Bourke-street,
Melb., was a very dull thoroughfare, as
compared with Collins-street, Buckley
and Nunn started operations in a tiny
shop, upon a bit of land leased from
a potato-dealer, whose tubers they
helped to cart away. Farmer coming
over from Sydney in search of Mel
bourne “investments” was struck by
the idea that these young beginners
were likely to make a bit. Negotia-
tions between the parties resulted in
Farmer buying the land round about
the infant store, and building a con-
siderable portion of the present Buck-
ley and Nunn premises, at a total cost
of <£20,000. The rent of the new “em-
porium ” was : fixed at .£3OOO, and the
first year’s trading therein yielded a
profit of .£20,000. In due course Buck-
ley and Nunn took the property over
from their Sydney landlord, who had
done so well for his proteges, and by
them. Fanner it was who first saw
the large business possibilities of the
Bourke-street site; but the success of
Buckley and Nunn was hastened by
their own smartness in advising another
young draper to open a rival shop next
door to them and so help to make a.
market. The business known as Robert-
son and Moffatt’s started four month*
after Buckley and Nunn’s, and during
the next 40 years the opposition firms-
waxed fat together.
Theo. Fink and R. W. Best, who are

Parliament. J. G. Duffy and his late partner, J.

Westralian Leg. Assembly are Australian natives,

A definite announcement will soon be
made about the probable date of publication of the
late Alfred Stephen’s autobiography. The work
has been in hands over 15 years, and will con-
stitute almost a history of Australasia.

dead. Such men encounter the difficulties of a
Foj Chow missionary whose paid catechumens
said, “People tell us we speak Jesus’ words
because we eat Jesus’ rice.”

“ Suppose the news came that General
Booth had taken a young wife?” Well, The
Bulletin predicts that the news will come. Any
well-preserved old saint with such a hook-nose,
knuckly fingers, and glittering eye, is sure to
take a wife—a young wife.
“Handsome Charley Lett” is still in full
force at the Imperial Institute. His strong point
at present is N.S. Wales meat, which he sells
direct from the Institute. His Australian friends
now mostly drop around the corner when they see
him coining, for fear of receiving a butcher’s bill.

course, travels free) was asleep in a corner of the
Leichhardt (Sydney) tram the other night, when
the conductor woke him up and demanded his
ticket. “Do you mean to tell me,” indignantly
exclaimed H. Parkes, “ that you don’t know who
lam ?” Blasphemy !

It was the largest funeral ever given to a Melb. clergy-

There are lots of men remaining in Melbourne
who deserve pretty large funerals. The Age has
a neat turn for compliments.

The luck of 13 again. A Sydney girl, the
youngest of 13 children, was born on 13th May.
Her mother died when this girl was 13 years old,
and she married a worthless sweep in that same,
her 13th, year. And she stood it for 13 years,
until the wretch shot himself by mistake for her.
So she had some luck, after all. But what’s ahead
of that girl ?
They call ex-Speaker Bent “the Cheerful
Giver ” in Melbourne now, because he so cheer-
fully gave his 36,000 shares, with a .£9OOO call on
them, to M‘Mahon, and left him to be sold up for
the liability. The title is derived from that text
which says that “the Lord loveth a cheerful
giver.” This is the only time on record when the
Lord has loved Tom Bent.
Vic. millionaire Clarke is an excellent
landlord —as good even as Burdekin, of Sydney.
There is not a poor man on his estates around
Creswick and Ballarat, writes a Bulletin corres-
pondent. All are well-to-do and occupy splendid
residences, erected by the owner. The rent paid
is extremely moderate, and many tenants would
offer double before giving up the land.

G rose, who unseated Richardson, late Vic.
Rail A ny Minister, at Oreswick, was, after the elec-
tions, introduced by Peacock to Shiels. The latter,
whose language is always eloquent and free,
warmly congratulated Grose on ridding the House
of the sanctimonious Richardson. “Yes,” con-
tinued Shiels, with numerous colored interpola-
tions, “you never know when you’ve got these
Wesleyan preachers; you can never trust
them.” The volatile ex-Premier wondered at
Grose’s silent acceptance of his congratulations,
but, later on, when Peacock informed him that
Richardson’s successor was himself a Wesleyan
devil-whacker, Shiels understood.

Who will be the next Victorian Chief
Commissioner of Police in succession to Mr.
Chomley, the very best and most popular chief
of police any Australian province has ever
had ? About six of the officers next in
rank to Mr. Ohornley are due, by the operation
of the sexagenarian rule, to depart about
the same time, so that the new man would
be heavily handicapped. If a “ranker”
were selected, it would have to be Supt.
Parkinson, of Ballarat; but, as a pro-
nounced Orangeman, he would be hardly
suitable besides, he must retire 15
months after Mr. Chomley. Penal
Inspector - General Evans is going
strong for the post, but is not
officially favored. The difficulty
will probably be surmounted
by the retention of Mr.
Chomley for a further term,
during which his successor
might be selected and trained.

Old George Bates, who had lived on
Kangaroo Island since 1824, which is 12 years
before the first Governor arrived in S.A., was
admitted to Adelaide Destitute Asylum, Oct. 9.
He. is 95 years old, but still able to talk clearly
concerning the scenes witnessed in his boyhood.
Much indignation is felt at the action of the
Govt, in carrying him away from his old home,
but the authorities aver that he could not have
been properly treated on the island.
Thus perorated N.S.W. Chief Jus.me
Darley, himself an enhandled personage :
Yes, the Right Honourable Sir Alfred Stephen is dead,
and New South Wales may well mourn this day.” . . .
His Honor (adds S.M. Herald) was visibly affected while
making this deliverance, and almost broke down before
concluding.
Which touches the very climax of bathos. “The
Right Honourable Sir Alfred Stephen is dead !”
Why didn’t Darley, C. J., say, while he was about
it: “Yes, the Right Honourable Sir Alfred
Stephen, P.C., G.C.M.G., &c., is dead”? It
would only have been one whit more pompously
absurd.
South Australia’s oldest public man died
Saturday, Oct. 13, in the person of John Dunn, of
Mount Barker, founder of the big milling firm
bearing his name. Born in 1802, he arrived in the
province in 1840, was elected to the first Parlia-
ment under representative government in 1857,
and, after eleven years’ service in the Assembly,
transferred his affections to the Council, finally
retiring from politics in ’77. At one time he and
his two sons all sat in the Legislature together.
He had little education, but plenteous energy, and
his fortune stands in six figures. He married,
when past 70, as his second wife, a sister of the
spouse of his son, but the only child, despite the
fact that it was baptised in water from the river
Jordan, did not survive. He built a windmill
unaided in ’4l, and made the patterns for the
machinery with his own hands. Then he humped
them 20 miles to Port Adelaide, and sent them to
Sydney to be cast, afterwards carrying the iron-
work back on his shoulders to Hay Valley. He
never wearied of telling the friends of his old age
how hard he worked when he was young. During
the whole of his 92 years he never smoked, drank,
or swore, reserving all the energy which would
have been devoted to those vices to assisting in
the task of living to 100, an age he was very am-
bitious of reaching. Till within the last few
months he had continued hale, hearty, hard-
working, and cheerful, but at the beginningof the
winter he began to break up, and the last
unseasonable weather finished him. He was short,
stout, and waddlesome, but otherwise of splendid
physique. This is printed for the benefit of The
Bulletin’s readers not in South Australia. They,
poor wretches! —as explained in last issue—dread
John Dunn pars, more than they dread the Gawler
bunyip.
The privileges of a lawyer in Australia are
so extensive that one never takes up a paper with-
out hearing about something new in that direc-
tion. Recently, R. H. Levien, a Sydney lawyer,
made some offensive remarks about two witnesses
on the other side, and, by way of getting level,
thqpair followed him into the street and made
sundry remarks, equally offensive, concerning
him. Whereupon Levien tramped stolidly back
to the magistrate and complained, and the two
witnesses were fined ,£lO each for “ contempt of
court.” They didn’t make their remarks in the
court, and they didn’t make them about the court,
therefore, the only possible inference is that
Levien, or any other solicitor, is the court in his
own person, no matter where he may be, and that
contempt of Levien is an offence punishable by
law. AnyooJy who wants to feel contemptuous
about a solicitor—even if he is a newly-admitted
solicitor who hasn’t grown any hair on his face
yet—should make a note of this.

Major William Collard Smith, who was a
member of the Berry Black Wednesday Ministry
is dead. He was a genial old (volunteer) warrior'
and was chiefly known to fame by the jokes made
about his immense feet. The story about the
horse, unbroken to harness, at once allowing the
major to ride him on the ground that the major’s
feet irresistibly reminded him of shafts, i, as
become an Australian classic. Also, the major
when Minister of Education, spelled “until’’
with two “ l’s ” and “ recommend ” with one “ m.”

Purves, Q.C., who spoke, no doubt, hast ily
and merely in the fulness of his liver, should at
once have apologised to Surgeon O’Hara for in-
sinuating that the 1000 guineas he said he hud
received for an operation was for an illegal opera-
tion. For, had he said or written such a thing
outside court, the surgeon could have got from
him enormous damages, inasmuch as the accusa-
tion, if true, was one under which O’Hara might
have been sent to gaol for life.

Thirty years ago the late N.S.W. Chief
Justice Stephen was trying an alleged horse-thief
at Bathurst. Prisoner, who defended himself,
before addressing the jury, handed to the Judge,
through the sheriff, a neatly-bound “shilling
shocker” of the period, and thus prefaced ilia
speech: “Your Honor will find something to
interest you during my address to the jury, which
will necessarily be long and tedious. I regret
that the rules of court prevent me from entertain-
ing the jury with light literature.” He got off.

A CHEERING PROSPECT EOR SMOKERS.
A Swiss cigar manufactory claims to have dis-
covered a new method for treating tobacco prior
to its being rolled into cigars, by which the greater
part of the essential volatile elemetit, viz.,.nicotine,-
is released. By thus evaporating the tobacco in
vacuo, Messrs. J. Erossard & Co. have made a
cigar called Cavour, remarkable for its mildness
and aroma. It is specially suited for smokers in
hot climates. The Cavour cigar contains a smaller
percentage of nicotine than any other cigar, and,
consequently, can be indulged in without injurious
effect on the nerves. This new manufacture has
been approved of by the highest medical authori-
ties in Switzerland, and as the cigars are sold in
packets of eight for one shilling only, they ought
to become soon popular here.

Go wcu Evans, of the Argus, revolu-
tionises quicker than any of the politicians.
Forty of the present members of N.S.W.
Leg. Council are Parkes’ nominees. “Forty ! ”
“Tub Bulletin, that powerful stain on
the Australian character.”—lsis Advocate (Q.
nig. -labor organ).
Victoria has now a political “ Reform
Club.” So far, however, it has no political Per-
form Club.
Victoria’s “ defences ” cost her £216,000
a year. She is praying heaven to defend her from
her defences.
An absentee-tax was only popped into
Vic. Premier Turner’s manifesto at the last
moment—it had to be written in the printed docu-
ment, in fact.
Belgium has now got absolute manhood
suffrage, with compulsory voting. “ A call to the
polling-booth can no more be disregarded than a
call to arms.”
Fully 30 per cent, of the chemists and
druggists practising in N.S. Wales will be dis-
qualified for that occupation if the proposed Phar-
macy Bill be carried.
In superannuation, a lesson may be taken
from the Thespians. The £2O-a-week actor is
glad enough to take £5 later. The Civil Service
man can never step down, but only out. Why not?
It is said the N.S.W. “solidarity ” Ms.P.
pass all their spare time caUcussing. The pioneer
Labor Ms.P. tried the game, and nearly caueussed
each other to death. The Oauoussian soon gets
played out.
, Sargood says that one reason why he insists
upon imported officers for the Vic, “army”
is that the elevation of local warriors would lead
to “no end of jealousy.” The' Bulletin suggests
t(ie advisability of importings a British Minister of
Defence for the reason that no local person with
horse-sense seems to be available.
“ Her Majesty heaped on him the highest
honors in her bestowal,” said Griffith, C.J. (Q.),
in adding his portion of slobber to the deceased
N.S.W. ex-Judge Stephen’s little, pile, “and he
valued them, as every good man does.” Now,
Queen Victoria didn’t give Judge Stephen “the
highest honors in her bestowal,” and in talking as
above the Northern political Judas is only apolo-
gising for his own pitiful personal position.
Victoria’s butter-
export to England this
year will be valued
oyer £1,000,000. Yet
the butter-trade is
moitly of “ artificial ”
origin, in the good old
Freetrade phrase. The
trade was a miserably
poor affair for many
years, and at l£tet it
nearly flickered out.
Then the Vic. Govern-
ment started it afresh
with an export bonus,
and even with that the
Agricultural Depart-
ment had to collect
the first season’s cargo
and supervise its ship-
ment ; in fact, the busi-
ness was dragged into
eixistence by main force.
Yet in five years it
grew to be one of the
biggest things in Vic-
toria, and the fact
that an indmtry can
be artificially created
and yet be a stupendous
success is demonstrated
bpyond dispute. More-
over, Victorian butter
is now cheaper that it
ev ir was before to the
Ipoal consumer, becau.se
it is made in vast quan-
tities by the best
labor-saving apparatus;
and it is also better
because the British
buyer wouldn’t stand
the old kind of pro-
duct, and it improved
as a matter of neces-
sity. An effort is now
being made to create a
similar “artificial ” in-
dustry in cheese, and
as the export has ex-
panded in 18 months
from about £3OO worth
tp nearly £20,000
Worth this trade also
promises well.
; Nothing rilore conclusively proves the
value, for party purposes, of the possession of a
flight to a dissolution than Dibbs’ nagging at Reid
dn the subject. A Premier who holds a power to
Absolve is practically a dictator. All the small-
in Parliament flock, to him at the sound
of his whistle. Parkes’ first remark to a friend
when Carington once endowed him with this
power was : “ I feel now' like Oliver Cromwell!”
Squatter Riddoclq M.P. (S.A.), so often
speaks of “ my class ” privately and publicly, that
he is nicknamed “My Class Riddocb.” Riddoch,
by the way, actually had the gall to openly blame
Geo. Hawker for voting with the Govt, on the
taxation resolutions, because “ the gentleman
must have known that the tax would prejudicially
affect his brother landowners,”
Justice limes, in sentencing at Dubbo
(N.S.W.) a number of rioters to terms of im-
prisonment, twice called a bush road “ the Queen’s
Highway.” Australia is so free and independent
that even its inland tracks belong to a foreigner
who never heard of them, never set eyes on Aus-
tralia, and only knows Australians by its worst
samples whom she degrades with knighthood.
Australian bush-tracks haven’t even got legal
names of their own.
Parkes believes he is making political
headway. He regards his Federation backing as
solid ; he considers he has posted himself as leader
of the Labor-party by pegging at Reid to make
the prohibition of the alien influx a question of
urgency, and he gathered a majority of the House
under him at the Local Option division. How to
invent a motion which will weld these three
parties to him is now the puzzle. The puzzle
solved, down goes Reid without benefit of clergy.
Good old Pastor Bevan wants the Con-
gregational Union to send a protest to France
against her threatened annexation of Madagascar,
and tell the Republic straight out that she is
“ doing wrong.” One never hears of a French or
German preacher sending a protest to the British
Government when it grabs Burmah, or Zululand,
or Egypt, or some other slice of territory. The
so-called “ Anglo-Saxon ” sin-shifter seems_ to be
the only one who has sufficient cheek for this kind
of foolishness.
Melb. Argus seeks to persuade the
workers against favoring the imposition of any
taxes on the rich on the ground that—
The more money is taken out of the pocket of the
individual citizen the less that man has to spend in the
employment of labour or in goods for his family.
As if capitalists -employ labour to benefit the
the labourers ! They don’t. They employ labour
to benefit themselves. Let the Argus talk like
this when next it is proposed to reduce wages in a
particular trade!
According to Brisbane Courier, repre-
sentatives of the Presbyterian Church lately
visited Mackay to report on the condition of the
Kanakas. They found IGOO Kanakas serving
under a re-engagement—“ thus proving that
Queensland has superior attractions to the
islands.” It may also prove that the Polynesian
doesn’t care to risk being landed on the wrong
island and eaten ; also, that he thinks he might as
well stay as be forced to return by the club of his
bribed chieftain.

of the ancient world than China does in ours ; but

was in Opposition, he announced publicly that iu

vindicated ” by one year’s imprisonment, and he
demanded his release from Premier Dibbs in the
emphatic terins; More than half his present
colleagues joined him in this demand. Now,
Premier Rddhas decided that Smith shall notbe
released until he has served three years of his
Btmtence , Therefore, Reid is either keeping in
gaol a man whom lie knows ought to be at liberty,
™ «!*> he formerly announced in the most absolute

knowing that he ought to : be iu gaol. In cither

have the courage to do what he hasn t the courage

The Yic. Employers’ Union has gone back
on the decision of its delegates, and refused to
experiment in the matter of arbitration, as was
agreed upon by representatives of Trades Hall,
Chamber of Commerce, and Employers’ Union.
The Union is content to leave the merits of its
case to the arbitrament of starvation.

D. J. Duggan, M.L.A., of Tarnagulla,
Vic., reminds his constituents, who, since his
return, have troubled him over much with the
extended hat: —
I was not returned to finance every institution in the
district out of its troubles. I propose to put the salary
allowed to me as your member into my own pocket, and,
further, I propose to endeavour to honestly earn that
salary.
Too many electors regard payment of members as
something in the nature of a municipal subsidy.
From a manifesto issued by the Australian
Socialist League :
There are two great classes of society. The one, the
capitalists, own land and Capital; the other, the workers,
own nothing except the power to labor.
The intermediary and greater class, the lions in
the path of Socialism, viz., the workers who own
something more than the power to labor, are con-
veniently lost sight of. Parkes said, in America,
that in and around Sydney a greater average of
workers own their own homes than in any other
town in the world.
Jenkin Coles, of S.A., proposes, as the
alternative to an increased land-tax, that the
eight-hour system should be abolished on the rail-
ways. By making every man work 10 or 12 hours
he reckons on saving .£25,000 a year and making
further taxation unnecessary. The idea was so
utterly foolish that no one even proposed to
listen while the out-of-date Jenkin was talking,
and when he had finished the Assembly walked
right over his mangled dust, so to speak, and
carried the Land-tax proposals by a good majority.
The old Tory party in S.A. is only a voice crying
aloud on an abandoned station these times.
The locomotives seemed to shake,
A tremor filled each vacuum-brake ;
The permanent way
Seemed festive and gay,
And so did every semaphore.
Grins broke out on the station clocks,
And merriment wreathed each signal-box,
The sleepers awoke,
Into laughter broke,
Making a regular railleryJroar
As, mile after mile,
Spread the railway smile
When Richardson was no more.
Molb. Argus publishes the screed of
“ Farmer’s Son ” —probably a member of the staff
—showing how a prohibitive tariff would naturally
“increase the producer’s burden.” The manu-
facturers, being relieved from all fear of outside
competition, might form a ring and raise the price
of every article by 100 per cent. It doesn’t occur
to the woolly-headed freetrader that protection
from outside competition must inevitably stimu-
late inside competition. This “ring” theory is
the thinnest of bogies. The result of a ring would
be a crop of new manufacturers, each striving to
be the first to capture the ring’s trade. Besides,
it is open to the public to form co-operative manu-
facturing companies for the destruction of any
grasping fools who attempted to plunder the
market.
Major-General Tulloch, in a recent address
in Victoria, said that the dear old mother-country
would some day avail itself of three regiments
raised in Australia to fight in Afghanistan. Is
the Sepoy getting tired of fighting his kinsman,
the Afghan, for his gracious Empress (whom ha
has never seen), or is he get ti"g tired of soldiering?

that it is “ conserva-

It insists that a Con-
servative cannot exist
in Victoria. They
have had a tough time
lately.
The chairman of a
Melb. hat factory, who
also happens to be con-
nected with the Free-
trade interest, objects
to having the success
of the hat-making busi-
ness credited to Pro-
tection entirely. It is
largely due to Protec-
tion, writes this funny
little man, but splendid
management has also
a Jot to do with it.
Then he (a mere figure-
head in the business,
anyway) observes that
“ with tlie same energy
and forethought ap-
plied to other indus-
tries now languishing
in the colony there is
little doubt but that
they could be made
equally successful.”
Which, coming from
a boon companion of
importers, is good
enough to treasure in
the memory. A very
heavy duty on hats
has enabled the Mel-
bourne factory to
flourish with the assist-
ance of careful manage-
ment, and the same
wise administration
would do likewise for
other industries —in
their present state of
Protection. But not
if the Government re-
duces their Protection
on the Free trade
ground that they
haven’t achieved “ the
hoped-for results.” Ad-
vocates of a 25 per
cent, maximum will
have to chew the letter
of this hat - factory
chairman when Tariff
Reform comes before
the Assembly.
The Marquis of Qneeusberry lost his son
last week by a trim-accident, and now it is reported
that the young wife he married the other day is
petitioning fur a decree of nullity of marriage.
The Sydney railway extension from Red-
fern to Cir ular Quay must come sooner or later,
but when the line is made it should be done by
tunnelling the entire distance. Tunnelling is ex-
pensive, certainly, but not one-tenth as expensive
as the resumption of pri vate,property according to
the fraudulent plan which now prevails. Nearly
every person who now advocates the Circular
Quay extension is interested in property on the
way, which he proposes to sell to the State at six-
teen times its value, and nearly everybody who
violently advocates some particular route for the
line does so because his own little tenement lies on
that route, and lie is afraid lest the line may go
in some other direction and he won’t be “re-
sumed.” Highway robbery and garrotting are
honest employments compared with the resump
tion of land for public purposes.

Between fill living men and the next
world, if there is any next world, stands a wall
through which no man has seen or can ever see.
He who believes that he can interview, through
any medium whatsoever, the ghosts of the
departed, is mad or on the verge of madness. If
you think you see a ghost, hit it on the skull with
a half-brick. Or, if you can manage to administer
to the spook of “ Cissie,” who likes lollies, a
chocolate-cream containing a stiff dose of jalap or
croton oil it will be equally efficacious in burst-
ing up “ the circle.”
Spiritualism is a delusion, the methods whereof
have been exposed a thousand times in a hundred
different countries. There are living scores of
men who can, by avowed trickery,.perform all the
feats whereby the weak-brained and credulous
semi-epileptics of Sydney, the poor people with
goggle eyes and far-away stares, have lately been
duped. Regrettably incomplete as is the exposure
of the Sydney dodgery, compared with what
might have been, it will at least be the means of
confronting Mrs. Mellon;-in any future Australian
seances, with test-conditions under which she will
absolutely and ignominiously fail, even as every
other spiritualist similarly tested has absolutely
failed.
The Sydney Judge who was for long so ardent a
spiritist, has sustained a great shock through the
Mellon affair. His Honor doesn’t believe in Mrs.
Mjellon now, and is perfectly-astonished that she
could have been mixed up in such operations.
The hard-headed, porridge-eating Scotch doctors
of;-Sydney are now jeering at the dreadful anti-
climax in the scientific career of their impression-
able Irish professional .brother. You can’t humbug
a Scotch doctor with a black beard and a box of
chocolate-creams any more than you can with the
Marble Man.
• * *
M‘Neill, the roaring evangelist, who is
winning notoriety by a dexterous transfer to the
pulpit of the methods pf the sixpenny circus,
devotes special attention to Business Men. They
are the salt of the earth, and they have money ;
so: roaring evangelists invariably give them the
ton seat in the synagogue and the top line in the
bills. As Marcus Clarke inimitably puts it: —
Vou may work like a horse, and give all your goods to
fegd the poor, but if you ate not a Business Man you are
sounding brass or a tinkling cymbal. To be a Business
Man is a special gift -a sort of inherent virtue, like a cast
in jthe eye. If you are a Business Man you will succeed in
Business —that* is to.say, you will be a good husband, a
foiid father, a conscientious' Christian, always vote with
the Government, and when you die you will go straight
to<Heaven. If you are a Business Man, people will shout
for you, the papers Will write you up, and your friends
will give you prayer-books with gilt-edges.
M‘Neill is evidently a Business Man. So is
.M‘Neill’s god. - - \

A nasty knock for some people, adminis-
tered by the Howard Society of Victoria with a
petition for Mi's. Needle’s reprieve. “If the
di-ath penalty be the deterrent it is claimed to be,
t|en why do not onr public men and Our press
advocate theHianging of those fraudulent and
refined robbers of Melbourne, who have driven to
the grave hundreds of respectable citizens, and
temporarily ruined the whole country? If we
successfully deter from murder, are there not
,urgent reasons why we must similarly deter in
Melbourne from crimes of gigantic fraud which
threaten the very foundations of modern society?”
Herewith The Bulletin .begs to-advocate the
hanging of numerous ex-bank directors who have
•tie blood of self-slaughtered victims upon their
brazen (and, in many instances, bald) heads.
Perhaps the better plan would have been to invite
them all to tea with MaTtha Needle;

■ Nurses are bo -be ..charged £3 3s. for their
diplomas at”the“end of their course of midwifery
rii the State-supportedSydney-Benevolent Asylum.
Considering the advantage it is to the State to

sum from her.

Pentridge prison-made boots are becoming
very popular in Vic. public service. One building
is visited daily by an official prepared with atape-
measure, &0., to take the dimensions of the prison
patrons’ feet. Officers, high and Jaw, from
Public Service Commissioners to messengers, buy
the boots, the wearers of which are known by what
is happily called the “ Lirnbo limp.”

Do Card. Moran and Archbishop Carr
purpose backing up Papal Legate Satolli in ex-
cluding publicans from all R.C. religious societies ?
Also, brewers? Also, drinkers? If not, why
not? What essential difference exists between
the religions aspect of the Australian grog-
question and the American grog-question ?

The Elizabeth Bay (Sydney) case does not
argue in favour of arming the police. The
wounded watchman fired twice without effect,
and the constable who effected the arrest of the
alleged assailant seems to have had his revolver

N.S. Wales G.P.O. allows suburban
mail-boys 14s. a week for forage. Recently -an
inspector visited a post-office close to Sydney, and
found that the mail-boys (2) were not using their
horses Enquiry elicited that the animals were
in the paddock, and had been there six weeks and
a mqntlv respectively; the reasons assigned being,
that one boy was saving up for a new suit of
clothes, and the other was hard-up and wanted
some cash !■

lie Alfred Stephen’s objection to the

Much against bis own wish, but in defer-
ence to the opinion of several prominent Sydney
supporters, St. M‘Neill has allowed requests for
prayer to be made at some of his meetings.. As a
result, this part of the meeting is breezily
humorous. “For my mother-in-law” was the
despondent and soul-reaching wail of a sallow-
faced, hen-pecked man. “ For worldly ministers,”
was a fervent appeal, which M‘Neill clinched by
saying—“ probably brought .down by their con-
gregations.” The blatant shepherd ought to be
heartily sick of the “request ” business, produc-
mg, as it does, so many loathsome exhibitions of
transparent hypocrisy. v

The uselessness of an editor is strikingly
illustrated by Miss Constance Slay Macnamara,
a Rue Australian young liiciy, whose pa (formerly
of South port, Q., Bulletin) now runs a Para-
guayan paper. “ Father doesn’t even know how
to drive a nail in straight,"’ says the ingenuous
Constance'May in a recent letter, “and mother
and I-dug holes in the,floor to put our bedposts
in.” So it was perhaps) just as well that Mae.
retired from Lane’s Eden soon after the creation.
1 he than who earns the contempt of his women-
folk by-inability to drive a nail in straight may be
able .to shift the earth with the Archimedean
lever, but he will never make a “ true mate ” in
practice. And the worst of it is that every other
editor is in the same fix. Not one of them would
miss his fingers five times in seven.

TO A FAIR ARSENIC-EATER.
The hues that tinge thy glowing skin,
Fair as a dawning summer's day,
And soft as roses’ petals are,
Flash but the radiance of decay.
’Tis not for this'l shun thine eyes,
Sweet! it is one to me
If God or-Devil gave those charms
So that those charms but be.
And, lest you think my swift retreat
Explaining dfd owe :
Sweet I you shall never be my wife' \
Lest you should be my widow.
D. W.
TO A MODERN HEBE
FOR THE BULLETIN.
You asked me if the Muses
I would invoke for you;
And who is there refuses
When lips so lovely sue?
Your eyes looked inspiration:
My lyre from silence woke,
Thrilled by the soft sensation,
And into music broke.
O maid more fair than Hebe,
.Cupbearer of great Jove
More beautiful than Phoebe,
When first she woke to love,
And on Endymion sleeping
Her sweet, shy kiss bestowed,
In,bliss his senses steeping
Until the cup o’erllowed.
Were you the goddess Luna,
And I the Latmian youth,
I’d think the frozen moon a
Bright land of love and truth ;
If Phoebus’ rays wore missing,
Love’s self should give the light;
We’ d see enough for kissing
Though it were always night.
You say you are not Phoebe,
Nor 1 Endymiou ;
But, though you may not she be,
Like her you’re to be won ;
Sure, such a lovely charmer . “
Will make some lover blest,
Ev’n if Diana’s armour
Should guard her beauteous breast.

N.S.W. Judge Wiudeyer remained fogged
for several days lately as to whether or not a
woman is citable as co-respondent in divorce.
The Judge’s wife and women righters generally,
all through the Madame Sterling fever, logically
insisted that women are and should be so citable.
“ Adventures are to the adventurous,”
said Disraeli—which is probably the reason why
Mr. J. J. Fitzgerald (whose portrait appears in
this week’s Bulletin) seems to have had so much
more than his share of peril on land and water.
When a boy, he accompanied Major Warburton
on his exploring journey ; later, he was one of the
party which brought the remains of Burke and
Wills back to Melbourne; and in 1875, at the
wreck of the Gothenburg in Flinders Passage,
between Bowen and Townsville (Q.), he
distinguished himself by special bravery in
life-saving. The Gothenburg ran on the
Perar reef in very dirty weather, and
remained for several hours apparently in no
danger ; but when the alarm had subsided the
captain put the engines astern, lifted the steamer
off into deep water, and she sank at once, drown-
ing 102 souls like rats. Mr. Fitzgerald’s conduct
received special notice from the Government and
people of South Australia. And now, at the
recent wreck of the Rodondo, off Esperance Bay
(W.A.), he gets high credit for courage and
presence of mind displayed in helping helpless
passengers.

* * *
Dear Bulletin, Your correspondent,
who pointed out the iniquities of the Australian
police, especially in re their brutal treatment of
inebriates, deserves gratitude. But let me draw
attention to how the police sometimes deal vvitli
helpless girls. An up-country hotel-keeper adver-
tised for a female cook. A decent Sydney
suburban girl applied and got the position. She
didn’t suit, however, and was “sacked” at the
end of the first week. With her wages, and a few
shillings, she railed to a township further inland.
In the carriage she made the acquaintance of a
young woman, to whom she related her troubles.
This young woman was going to the same town.
The very day the servant-girl arrived at the set-
tlement she searched for work in company with
her companion, who happened to be “ known to
the police.” Suddenly the local constable pounced
upon the girl, and, without warning or advice,
arrested her. A viciously-ignorant magistrate
gave her three months, as a person without visible
means of support. When her sentence expired
she walked to a town 20 miles distant, and got a
situation in a public-house as waitress. But a
police officer, who happened to have been present
when she was sentenced, found her out, and forth-
with informed the publican of her imprisonment.
Ho also gave her 12 hours to leave the town. The
moneyless and homeless girl went the way of
frailty—cruel, unromautie ruin. Such are some
of the guardians of the peace.—D.

It is doubtful whether
cheap railway excursions,
from the country to the
metropolis, pay the N.S. W.
railway authorities in the
long run. Every excur-
sionist returns home laden
with goods booked as “pas-
senger’s luggage ” and
carried free, which, but for
the cheap fares, would have
been bought in some
country town and, conse-
quently, have paid freight.
The big Sydney shops alone
profit by the system, at
the expense of the small
country store-keepers.

Chosen, 35 per cent, of the

“ Were you very much gone on the de-
fendant?” a fair plaintiff was asked in a recent
Charters Towers (Q.) assault case. “I was very
much gone on him, but not dead gone on him,
she answered dexterously, and the lawyer s hopes

# • *
The undertaker’s Journal, In Meaioriam,
recalls a society that once existed in Melbourne to
combat the silly custom of costly funerals.
Marcus Clarke, Andrew Semple, Jardine Smith,
and other Yorickers, were prominent members or
it—but they were all buried in more or less expen-
sive style.” The only way in which any real
reform can come about in this direction would be
for the State to become undertaker to the com-
munity. Uniformity and economy could then be
achieved.

The phrase “cleared out” {i.e., “at
the Customs ”) is but one of a host of nau-
tical expressiosn which have taken fast root
in colloquial English. Some of these are patent
enough; but how many people know that they
are “talking sailor” when they say “ threw me
over,” “hard-up,” “taken aback,” &c. ? Of
course it’s a question of drawing the line, but the
line keeps ever shifting, and it’s long odds that
even Justice Wiudeyer has used a certain familiar
metaphor connected with clearing a ship of water.
TO THEOSOPHISTS.
(Respectfully dedicated to Mrs. Besant.)
Yo talk of wisdom new and wisdom olden,
Ye say the heirloom of your mystic band
Is that great key that opes the portal golden
Of wondrous astral world and spirit land ;
Ye peep and peer until beneath your vision
Bi raphic figures rise and fade and fall;
Ye, boast ye lift the veil of life Elysian
And read the writings in the Judgment Hall.
Naught know we of your soarings all supernal,
Your worlds of wonder and your deeps divine;
We plumb no seas profound of Truth Eternal
With moonbeam-woven span-long sounding-line.
’Tis yours to solve the problem of Existence,
And ours its daily dues to understand ;
’Tis yours to dream what dimly lies in distance,
Ours—well, to do what clearly lies at hand.
P. LUFTIG.

A curious incident happened at the wreck
of the Rodondo. When the last boat left the ship
One of the passengers was observed to look
anxious and feel hurriedly in his pockets. Then,
without a word, he jumped over the side of the
boat and swam strongly towards the ship, amid
loud cries of “ Come back !” He reached the ship,
clambered up the side, and disappeared, while the
boat waited in suspense. Presently the man’s
face was seen again above the bulwarks; the.boat
put in close to the sinking vessel; and he was
hurriedly dragged aboard. Not for some time did
the others think of asking him what he risked his
life to secure. Then the man eloquently opened
his eoat and showed a bottle of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, the greatest nerve and brain restorer of.
the age.


Musical compositions are like diamonds of
all qualities. H. J. Samuell’s popular publica-
tions are of the first water. They are purchased
frequently to send to friends as souvenirs of Aus-
tralian art. They consist of Juncker’s “I Was
Dreaming,” a pretty song, made famous by Miss
Nellie Stewart; Leon Caron’s famous waltz “I
Was Dreaming,” to be played at the Melbourne
Cup by Zelman’s Military Band; Leon Caron’s
Nitouche galop, from the opera “Matn’zelle
Nitouche;” Harrison White’s “Slumber Song,”
sung by Miss Colbourne Baber and Miss Isabel
Webster, with great success; “My Wedding
Day,” Leon Caron’s latest pretty song, sung- by
Mi s Tastna Sherwin ; Mme. Kellermann’s lovely
“ Duchess of York Waltz ; ” the “ Nautoh Song,”
by Leon Caron, from “Mam’zelle Nitouche.” To
be obtained of all music-sellers. Two shillings
each. H. J. Samuell, publisher, Box 1077, C.P.O.*

My Dear Moobabinda—
The theatrical carnival, at Sydney Association
Ground, last Thursday, was one of the liveliest
affairs ever set out under the bright blue sky,
despite the awful cigar-smoke—for, though the
real johnnies bought the fearsome carnival cigar
and gave it away, some of the purchasers con-
sumed them there and then with deadly effect.
Still, I liked the carnival, lock, stock, and barrel,
perhaps because, as an unprotected female, the
vendesses of smokes, matches and boutonnieres
left life unplucked. It was undoubtedly the men’s
day out, and I would like to pat ’em on the back
for the way they met the occasion. Not a man
but was beflowered, and every man’s hand was
always either going into or coming out of his
breeches’ pocket. The failure of the show, from
a dress-circle girl’s point of view, was certainly
the absence of a Brownlow tent. The massive
Wah ace, dressed as the wicked Henri, ladling out
mugs of “ Rosette ” milk, would have been turn-
ing money away in no time—but, as I said before,
it was the men’s day out.
Miss Nellie Stewart had the largest tent, spas-
modically decorated with potted shrubs and flags,
and when the fun got fast and furious you paid
one shilling merely for the privilege of entering.
This gave the beauteous prima-donna something
1 1« complexion of a monstrosity-show, but it
helped to keep the tent cool. Miss Stewart’s car-
nival frock consisted of a slim tlack skirt with
blouse bodice; of grey-ancl-white checked silk
trimmed with pale-blue ribbons and lines of black
lace edged with cream. The Nellie Stewart
bangle was in evidence, and it must now be the
proper caper to wear your watch on a long, slen-
der. old-fashioped gold chain going three times
round the neck, since Nellie wore her time-piece
in that way. A toque hat with bows of checked
ribbon, a crimson poppy and sprays of currants,
topped off the only Nellie’s braided locks, and she
wore a veil, of. course, as they all did. Gilded
youths and society maidens hung on her smile,
and I can assure you I heard a girl say to Nellie,
in tones of deepest tenderness : “I want a cup of
t»a, please, but oh, do give it to me yourself.”
Among all the unbridled adulation, a large,
hairy man, who was rushing violently to and fro
with crockery ware in Nellie’s tent, came quite as
a refreshing change. Surrounded by Nellie her-
self, Florence Young (in white pique and a pink
shirt, with a cash receptacle like a bookmaker’s
bag), Miss Deorwyn (in a pale brown gown,
trimmed with brown moire), and Nina Osborne,
the unemotional creature —I mean the hairy man-
scorned to recognise the delirious joy and ineffable
privilege of his position. As he swept up a bat-
talion of used dishes he said, loudly and im-
patiently, “ If they’d get out of my way, I could
work. I can’t work with women !!! ” It is quite
evident that this outspoken gentleman will never
give his countenance to the petticoat vote.
Mrs. Tapley’s tent was a great attraction.
Violet wore a crisp frock of heliotrope crinkled
with fandangoes of guipure lace and black
rosettes like full-stops all about. Her hat was
trimmed with black violets, and her smile went
on for ever. Hubby Tapley, who always looks
like Tommy Merton off the stage, hovered near
to protect his bride from the hard-held compli-
ments of the male multitude; and May Pollard
(in white and a pale-blue sash) and Mrs.
Lauri (in a grey check suit and a shirt front) lent
a hand in the Varley interest.
Kate Bishop (Mrs. Lohr), in black silk and
moire, had a society-position in the members’ tea-
room and did a roaring trade in light refresh-
ments. Her specialties were melty little cakes
called “kisses,’ of which (unlike the real article,
thank goodness) the supply wasn’t equal to the
demand. The handsome Mona Rathbone, of
tableau vivant fame, assisted Mrs. Lohr in dis-
pensing shilling cups of tea with the air of a
Melpomene.
There was an uncountable crowd in both the
stands, and it looked as if everybody was there.
A well-known Melbourne lady attracted most
attention by the acrobatic manner in which she
balanced a Paris hat shaped something like a
tablespoon-on-end at the extreme back of her
golden head ; but there was nothing else uncon-
sciously remarkable on view except a white-faced
girl shrouded in a bright blue veil which lent a
gruesome, corpse-like phosphorescence to her pallid
complexion.
The programme was almost entirely comic. It
is the correct thing to ask for attic salt only with
dishes of this sort, but I couldn’t help laughing at
a menagerie race with a spotty pig tearing along
in almost workmanlike manner with his curly tail
straight out and winning by lengths from a
wallaby, a oat, and a brace of roosters. The only
item I didn’t like was the ladies’ bicycle-race.
The contestants were properly gotten up in
knickerbockers and hose with Directoire coats and
little caps, and the pace was very merry all round,
but I can’t think such furious pedalling is healthy
for women. They all wheeled round the ring in
dashing style, but I saw one of them when the
glory was over, holding herself together with both
hands in the seclusion of the members’ stand,
with a face the color of cigar-ash.
To “sum up,” as the Judges do, the theatrical
carnival is just the sort of show Sydney stands in
need of. Plenty of music, plenty of people, lots
of harmless merriment, and no finnick. One
hears, of course, tea-table whispers of So-and-So’s
going home with Miss Longstockings, while his
wife “waited hours on the grand-stand for him,
my dear.” But, then, men have the happy knack
of getting into mischief anywhere, and I don’t
know why we should expect better things from a
theatrical carnival than we do from a church
choir.
There was Roman dressing in “ The Mounte-
banks,’’ which scarcely filled the bill at Sydney
Lyceum for the last six nights of the comic opera
season. I always think “The Mountebanks"
must have been written to give the coryphees a
chance to mend the Jacob’s ladders in their long
stockings, for many a pink limb points the way to
Heaven in this operetta, and the only ballet it
boasts always reminds me of a Bowdlerised version
of the temptation of St. Anthony. A semi-circle
of cowled monks in the background look on un-
moved while a scurry of village girls, in parlous
long skirts, wdiirl and jump like a Sunday-school
picnic with a bull-ant in its stockings, and I don’t
wonder the Brotherhood remain unimpressed, for
the dance isn’t half as thrilling as a line of assorted
washing on a moderately breezy day.
Miss Nellie Stewart’s Teresa seemed a nice sort
of girl in a Roman apron, but after a long and
welcome season of Nellie the first unreasoning
adoration wears off and you begin to take notice
that every picture in her stage gallery (with the
sole exception of Yum Yum) has long blonde hair.
In this era of peroxide of hydrogen one is apt to
look slantwise at the artless girl who shakes her
butter-color tresses in the breeze ; nevertheless,
Nellie (who is always wigged and cannot plead
the Bernhardtian excuse of playing in her own
hair) gives us a study in straw-color nearly every
time. I believe that lots of people at the fete ex-
pected to be given shilling cups of tea by an ador-
able Nellie emerging out of a whirl of sun-kissed
locks, and were left gasping for breath when a
demure personage, with light-brown hair braided
in tight plaits, handed them their potion instead.
Violet Varley, in Flora Graupner’s old part,
made a very dolly Ophelia in a Niagara of flaxen
hair, and was warmly welcomed by the audience.
She shared the evening’s fioralities with Miss
Stewart, whose tribute was in the form of a few
floral rings, which the lady doubtless took to
mean that she is only four rounds from the top of
the ladder of art. But I hope it wasn’t meant as
a foreshadowing of the gangway up which Nellie
(I hear) intends to trip, after the Melb. opera-
season is over, on her way back to have another
shot at British laurels. It isn’t pleasant to be
reminded that Nellie hasn’t come to stay.
As the rattling rantipole of the Tamoires,
Wallace Brownlow was a disappointment.
Whether it’s the fell effects of the Beautiful
Harbor (which Evangelist M'Neill blames for his
loss of vocal vigor—may the Beautiful Harbor be
for ever blessed !—and which may be the reason
why no soprano hails from its shores) or merely
that sustained effort has found his throaty baritone
out, I don’t pretend to know, but Mr. Brownlow
hasn’t enough voice to hail a ’bus with at time of
writing. But what a consistent and reliable per-
former Mr. Tapley is! Always nicely dressed
and painted, and going bang through his music
note for note, he must be a very treasure to the
management.
Ex-Ohief Justice Stephen 1 always regarded as
a social diplomat to the end of his finger-nails. A
forward and pushing woman aspiring to society
honors, and only on nodding terms with the
courteous old man, boldly tweaked him by the
sleeve one day at a very swell gathering and
begged him (although she had to recall both her
face and name to his memory) to introduce her to
Lady Blank. Now, the ex-Ohief Justice hadn’t
the remotest idea “who” the candidate was, and
yet, as she was apparently in the swim, he hardly
knew how to refuse. So, turning to Lady Blank,
he said : “ Allow me to present Mrs. Dash to you,
Lady Blank. I have not had the pleasure of
dancing with her, but that probably is my mis-
fortune.” On the spur of the moment, and for a
man of 91 (it only happened last year), I don’t
think this gentle hint that he didn’t back up his
introduction could well be improved upon. At
any rate, Lady Blank froze visibly.
The Australian agent, Mr. G. A. Falstedt,
sends me a sample of a new toilet wash yclept
“Amykos Aseptin,” which I can better recom-
mend to you in print than verbally, as I haven’t
the remotest idea how to pronounce it. A spoon-
ful of Amykos in a basin of water makes it delight-
fully sweet with a cinnamon scent that hangs
about one for a long time, and used as a mouth-
wash it really clears the throat and palate, besides
being a tooth and gum preserver and “ having a
destructive influence on all infusorise,” as the label
says. But, what is more to the point, “Amykos”
is all the label paints it to be, which is not the case
with all the gaudy and infallible toilet prepara-
tions that reach The Bulletin office.

former Parliaments ? If not, why is it that the
menu of the Parliamentary refreshment-room now
contains a new and significant nota bene. After
a statement at the head of the bill that soup with
joint or entree and sweets or cheese, also, tea comes
to “one-and-three” there is printed: “N.B.—
These charges apply to one helping only." To
what an apoplectic complexion must N.S.W.
legislators have already come I If a certain
statesman had found re-election there might have
been some well-understood reason for such a pro-
clamation, but, as it is, I suppose his knife and
fork have fallen on somebody.

, -A- small section of musical Sydney retired un-
impressed from a private recital given by August
W. Juncker at Paling’s on Monday afternoon,
when a new soprano, Miss Violet Ludlow, lifted
her voice to see whether Sydney would hail her as
a coming Nellie Stewart or not. Miss Ludlow, in
spite of being the prirna-donna of a band of New-
castle (N.S.W.) operatic amateurs, who make a
loud noise among the coals now and then, hasn’t
got quite enough voice to go ’Ome on, I am sorry
to say. When I first heard of Miss L. (who is
own cousin to Edward Lloyd, the English warbler,
by the way), I had hopes that, at last, a N.S.W.
soprano had risen in the east—but that pheno-
menon isn’t going to happen just yet. Miss
Ludlow sang Herr Juncker’s nevv song, “All in
All. The song is a little in a groove, perhaps,
with much sostenuto, d la “I Was Dreaming,"
but the words, by E. Long-hair Scott, are prettily
put, and the music wraps itself round them like
crape round a widower’s hat.
News of Mrs. Mellon and her spooks has, I
hear, been received with great interest in Mel-
bourne, where she was made famous by Prof.
Laurie’s lecture, satirised by Rev. G. Walters
as ‘Six Months of Psychical Research, by One
Who Wasn t There.” The Age got in first
with the exposure (despite Mr. David Syme’s
eerie predilections), but the Argus made a specialty
of it two days after with a Sydney Sunday Times
article, published from advance sheets.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
A brief analysis of the Caulfield Cup chances in
last week s Bulletin was rational, in so far that it men-
tioned only nine candidates by name. Of these nine,
tillee Ruenalf, Steadfast and Moscow —did not start.
The others were Levon, Bruin, Capstan, Tarcoola, Paris
and Tellus, so the six mentioned runners actually included
the three placed horses. Wonderful tipping 1 Out of 26
unplaced o ms, the haughty prophet ignored 23? True,
the paragraph affirmed, firstly, that Bruin would beat
Devon if he could stay the course—secondly, that Ruenalf
ought to hold Paris safe, and Tarcoola should heat all the
heavy-weights-thirdly, that the writer would follow
Capstan if supported by the stable. But Bruin didn’t stay,
Ruenalf didn t run, and Capstan,was not “supported” by
the stable, or even by the prophet’s five shillings, for
that matter. Nothing in the par. was absolutely inexcus-
able, save the suggestion of Tarooofa beating top-weight
Paris. That was the one blot on the analysis. The fore-
going remarks are intended for gall-pure gall.
By the time this appears much will have been
written about the victory of Paris, for the second time, in
Caulfield Cup—“his marvellous performance under a
crushing weight,” &c. Paris is a good horse who can do
a certain thing particularly well. He has annexed eight
events during the last five years, handicaps all, or nearly
all, of them. One was a mile race, three were 11 furlongs,
and four were 12 furlongs in distance. A somewhat better
stayer than the common “flyer,” Paris can eorae with his
brilliant rush at the end of 1£ miles. He’s a specialist.
He has never fought out a finish with a first-class horse.
Note that runners-up to Carbine in weight-for-age and
handicap races were Abercorn, Loohiel, Melos, Dread-
naught, Megaphone, Highborn (to whom he gave
3s t. 101 b.), and The Admiral, some of these
playing second fiddle on more than one occasion.
The runners-up to Paris have been Doncaster,
Helene, Halo, Norbert, Candour, Yarron, Aureus and
Devon. Paris has shown that He can win up to 1J miles,
nor is he particular to a few pounds avoirdupois. In two
Melbourne Cups, a Metropolitan (2 miles), a Canterbury
Plate and a Sydney Cup this much “ messed-ahout ” little
horse has cut a by no means brilliant figure.
Previous to each of his Caulfield Cup wins,
Pans has run nowhere -in the Caulfield Stakes, a week
earlier. This year he was badly beaten in the Stakes
(9 furlongs) by two unbacked three-year-olds and Euroka
—at weight for age. Yet the V.A.T.O. didn’t call for any
explanation of the horse’s inconsistent performances.
There may have been an explanation, but why, in the
name of sporting decency, wasn’t it called for?
Devon and Bruin ran in the Caulfield Cup true
to their form in the Toorak Handicap. Bruin,
when giving 121 b. to Devon over a mile, had looked to be
winning until his heart, or his strength, suddenly failed.
Bruin, when meeting Devon at even weights over the
H miles seemed to be winning much more easily than
before, but he stopped as before and Devon beat him
again. It would seem that the second and third horses in
the Cup must have conspired together to make the
winner’s sudden improvement in “form” seem “un-
accountable.”
In next week’s issue The Bulletin tipster
will try to pick the winner of the Melbourne Cup, and
show cause why a lot of fancied horses should not win in
any case. This particular tip shall be a four-year-old,
unless more than two animals of that age go amiss in the
meantime.
Ruenalf was scratched for Caulfield Oup at the
tenth hour, for the alleged reason that his owner couldn’t
back him at a fair price, or “get any of the sweep
money.” The “cleverness” of this scratching policy
needs finding. A horse can’t remain at concert-pitch
week after week, anyway. If Ruenalf was a moral cer-
tainty for the Caulfield Cup, the owner might as well have
backed him to win a comfortable fortune at a price no
shorter than he will have to take for the Melbourne Cup,
if he intends to spin his horse for that. And, considering
how a “dead bird” for the Caulfield may be quite in-
capable of winning the longer race against a better field,
Mr. Kays is likely to find that he has thrown away a good
deal of Caulfield substance for a Flemington shadow. As
for sweep-money, it is to be hoped that the drawers of
Ruenalf for the Melbourne Cup will be situated in remote
spots, where they can’t be got at.
Indigration
may be the result of a variety of causes—debility
of the digestive tract, over mental work, nervous
prostration, excessive smoking, tea or spirit
drinking. At any rate, no matter what the cause,
the only treatment of any permanent benefit is a
restorative treatment; the remedy must possess
tonic, invigorating and strengthening properties.
This is not the case with any of the many purging
mixtures sold as dyspepsia panaceas, and this
is proved by their makers stating that the dose
must be increased until the desired effect is
attained. This is due to the nostrum being
purely a cathartic, and by continued use the
stomach, liver, and bowels become educated to the
action of the medicine and it gradually loses all
effect. Clements Tonic is quite distinct in its
action, it assists digestion, promotes the flow of
gastric and pancreatic fluids, increases the secre-
tion of bile and thus assists the bowels to throw off
the waste matter; it strengthens the nerves, forms
bone, muscle and nerve tissue, and increases the
appetite. Clements Tonie is a natural restorative
of a debilitated system. Read the case of Mr.
William W. Brown, Inspector W, and S. Board,
Cowrie-street, Newtown, who writes on Dec, 28th,
1892 : —For about two years I have been a severe
sufferer from biliousness and indigestion, with con-
stant attacks of dizziness; in fact, I have been at
times incapacitated from following my daily avoca-
tions as a Government Inspector. At times when
seized with dizziness I have had to sit down to
keep myself from falling. Hearing so much about
the nerve-strengthening qualities of Clements
Tonic, I resolved to give it a trial, which I have
done with the most beneficial results. I had only
taken two bottles when I,found myself thoroughly
cured. l am only too happy,to add my testimony
to the number already received.—Wishing you
every success j and a happy now year, I remain,
yours gratefully, William W. Brown, Inspector
W. and 8. Board, No. 5 (Cowrie-terrace, Cowrie-
street, Newtown, N.S.W.

Martin ® our^e ) killed last week by a fall in
schooling,” had almost equal claims with Tom Com ■•an
to be the most popular man in Australia.” He was a
veteran at the game, with a solid reputation for honesty
and a more unassuming jockey never lived. Also, he was
the hero of about a dozen awful tumbles that were sup-
posed to he fatal at the time. His capacity for getting
bones broken was a standing joke among his mates. He
went to England and rode a winner over the sticks fot
the Chi resides. In South Australia he was almost in-
vincible fora time. In Victoria he had a special reputa-
tion for accepting dangerous mounts, whereas Corn-an
was associated with some of the “softest things” goinu
Luck was not kind to poor old Martin Bourke in Victoria
but he never failed to come to the fronc when he cot a
fmr chance, and two of his wins in the Flemington Grand
Nat. Steeplechase were “surprises.” In 1891 he drove
the plodding Crusoe home through the mud. This year
he dm the trick again with Daimio, the hunter. The
public don t like suprises, however, and so Bourke's name
was not adored in the land. He had not received a
great ovation" since 1888, when he won the G.N.
Steeple, on Eaglet, for the Millers, and completed their
double which Redleap (always ridden by Coxj had
begun. Bourke’s homely Irish countenance will be much
missed by the older school of racegoers.
