tance, challenging the Melb. Cup, which is stationary, or
retrograde. The night before the Caulfield battle, just
over, was very nearly as lively in Bourke-st. as a Cup eve.

virtuous writer, boasted that he had taken a tenner to a

• • • *
In 92, Paris won the Caulfield Cup, carrying
Bst. 81b. in 2.38 J. On Saturday, when he won it a''ai.i,
he cut off the quarter second with his 9st. 41b. up.
• • • *
Devon’s meritorious performances at Caulfield
bear out the now common saying that anything wiih
Musket blood in it can gallop. He is half-brother to
Occident, a Dunedin (M.L.) Cup winner.
• • * *
In picking an eleven to meet the Englisl mien
the N.S.W. Selection Committee has a tough task ahead.
N.S. Wales cricket should not be allowed to suffer in the
interests of sentiment. All things equal, the old hand
should obme before the colt; hut when it comes to
choosing between the old hand with tons of experience
and an ounce of cricket, and the youngster with tons
of cricket and ounce of experience, cricket should have
the call. Bannerman, Sid. Gregory and Moses have this
season, so far, shown nothing to justify their inclusion in
the team ; and, as regards the last named, the report that
he does not intend to play this season in first-class cricket
comes prematurely, as it has yet to he shown that he is
worthy of a place. The cry is usually raised that Banner-
man and Gregory should be played for their fielding
alone, but there are colts now playing who can field as
well as either of them when at their best. Turner is
bowling well, but is very much off in his baiting.
Although one department of the game usually suffers at
the expense of the other, the Bulletin always thought
“ Charley ” was going to prove the exception to the rule.
* • * ♦
The Blackham-Tooher quarrel has now been
amicably settled. Blackham’s remarks should have been
taken all along in a Pickwickian sense. The whole
trouble was assiduously fanned by a couple of Jonhny-
come-latelyg, people who were in the nursery when
the great wicket-keeper was fighting for Australian
cricket.
* * * *
Richardson, whom Stoddart is bringing out, is
reckoned England’s deadly bowler. The strong point
about his trundling is its remarkable effectiveness on a
hard wicket—a rare accomplishment for a bowler, and
bad news for our batsmen.
* » » *
S. Australia will put a strong eleven in the field
against Stoddart’s team, who open in Adelaide first
week in Nov. The backbone of S.A. will be G. Giffen,
Lyons, Darling, Reedman, A. H. and F. Jarvis, and Jones,
a very strong batting and howling seven. Walter Gitfen
will be omitted this time on his merits.
Fight-merchants do better than the actual
sloggers in the States. Griffo and M'Auliffe took about
£3lO each, as against £3IOO gathered in by the promoters.
Griffo’s stock has gone up higher in the States
than if the M'Auliffe decision had been in his favor,
and, at latest, he was rushed with offers of engagements.
Offers were thrown out for a finish go with M'Auliffe, and
a limited bout with Billy Plimmer, the little fellow who
bested Dixon. If the little Sydney tough keeps level-
headed—which it is a million to one he won’t—he should
now put together a competence at short notice.
♦
Prior to his defeat by Fitzsimmons, Creedon
claimed to have fought 111 times, with 109 wins and 2
draws. One of these was with Costello, whom he after-
wards beat.
* * • ♦
At latest, the Americans were trying to make
an actor of Griffo. The Bulletin suggests a piece—“ One
Hundred and Ten Nights in a Bar-room."
* * * •
A football match for a money-stake has been
played at Ballarat. Melb. papers call on the V.F.A to
prevent sueh “gambling contests." The policy of at-
tempting to pull up professionalism by the roots is
questionable. Cricket is an absolutely spotless game
beside football, and yet it admits professionals. Boxing
would be a tired art without the paid pug. Even sprint-
ing, with all its vices, gets something good from the pro.
What is wanted in professional football is not an evaded
(and evasive) “prohibition," but a “licensed” system.
Not rabid teetotalism, but temperance.
* ♦ * *
Frank Toms leaves Adelaide P. 0., at noon, on
Nov. 12, to break the cycling record between that city
and Melbourne. Snell lately did the journey in 3 days
6h. 15m. 15 secs.
* ♦ * ♦
Walter S. Kerr (amateur), Australia’s champion
bicyclist, ;was born at Doable Bay, Sydney, in ’7l, and is,
consequently, 23 years old. He stands sft. 6in. in height,
and in racing costume weighs 9st. He has been six years
on the track, and has held the championship for four
years. He also holds ail Australian records from one mile
to ten. Whence such a scrap of a man draws all his mar-
vellous go is a mystery. Perhaps, like the Yankee cyclist,
Ziminennann, he has an abnormally-large heart.

Woman’s Enfranchisement.
The Leaders in office all over the world, and
especially in this continent, are proposing that
woman, or at least some of her, should have the
suffrage extended to her. Very well then. This
action is partly the inevitable answer that rnust be
made to the voice ®f the people, especially as
election times are appppaching, and partly an
attempt to bolster up the iniquitous system of
plural voting which has received such a death blow
from the recent accession of workers’ representa-
tives to Australian Parliaments. _ Whether or not
the departure will prove productive of good or ill,
whether the enfranchised woman will neglect her
house duties and, vote for the ornamental candi-
date with the curly golden beard to the exclusion
of the rugged Hampden, or the patriot with odd
eyes, is a matter that rests in the womb of the
future. Woman’s political enfranchisement by
the march of progress may be an unmixed blessing
or an undoubted curse. Woman's disenfranchise-
ment from disease by the sterling virtues of Warner’s
Safe Cure can be nothing but a heaven-sent
boon. The great number of complaints from
which our mothers, the past builders of the world,
and our wives and sisters, who are to be the
mothers of the next generation, suffer, vanish
before ilui conquering remedies of H. H. Warner
& Co. Muscular weakness, tumors, abscesses and
all the nameless ills known generally as female
o miplaints, are evidences of blood corruption,
caused by the duties of maternity, or the errors of
dress, diet, and habits. It is the special duty of
the kidneys to keep the blood pure and capable of
doing its work, and when this function is impaired
only Warner’s Safe Cure can restore it. Patients
may reasonably expect great results from the use
of one or two bottles, but four or six bottles are
necessary, in some cases to produce permanent
results. But the results are so thorough that not
one of the thousands of Australian women thus
restored to the enjoyment of health would regret
the cost of even ten times the quantity mentioned
of the famous Safe Cure were such a quantity
necessary.

“Billy” Arnott, Maoriland’s champion pro.
hurdler, is now in Sydney “looking round.”
According to the late sporting baronet, Astley,
nuo-ilist Sayers would never, under any circumstances,
refuse a drink. Meeting him after the Heenan-Sayers
eombat, Astley remonstrated re his over-indulgence, but
Savers simply said: “ How many sorts do you think I ve
had between St. Marlin’s Church and Charing Cross?
“ I don’t know.” “ Well, I’ve just had 22."

exhibited at Hawkes Bay (M.L.) Show, the other day, but

going to win a big race but never did. The 2025-guineas

“ When the flag was at last lowered, a dull thud
nroclaimed that they were off.” This is not a report of a
hanging. It is part of Sydney Sunday Times’ description
of Caulfield Cup race.
♦ • * ’
Writes “ Victor For the second year in
succession “Carbine” Wallace seems to have fairly
thrown away the Caulfield Cup. Titan’s after-running
shows that the race would have been a good thing for him
last vear despite the penalty he incurred for winning
T mrak Handicap. This year, by trusting to Taupe
instead of Gaillardia, an equally egregious blunder has
been made.
Gaillardia (a stayer) would have finished far in
front of the non-staying Bruin, who managed, though
badly ridden, to struggle into third place. The form
shown by her in last season’s Newmarket Handicap run-
ning proves this. Let Bulletin readers keep their eyes
on the mare during Flemington meeting, where she is
heavily engaged and should account for one or more of
the prizes.
The Captain’s owner tried in vain to secure a
big bet about his horse, and, failing in this, he did not
send him across the Border. Hik horse will be to the fore
lit Xmas time. The Summer Cup will about suit him.
“No consultation-money to be had,” was the
ostensible reason for Ruenalf not running in the Caulfield
0 ip but all along it has been here predicted that the Cup
will be his journey. He will, moreover, very nearly win.
Tim Swiveller ran a good horse, and will beat
all the Caulfield Cup representatives in the Melb. event.
A few-more stfong gallops will do him no end of good.
Malachite’s running in the Maiden Plate
further enhances Cobbity’s form, for the latter can run
rings round him at home.
Between Moorite and Oobbity ’twill be found
the V.R.C. Derby will lie—the former for choice.
Had it nob been for Jack Fielder’s determined
finish on Paris, the other “ fielders ’’ would have had a
“ skinner ” over Devon, who weuldhave won with amore
experienced jockey up.
Lady Trenton sorely disappointed her multi-
tudinous backers. With a little weight off her back, she
will be very handy at the end of the year. Tattersali s
Cup?
The excellence of the Chester blood was again
exemplified by the wins of Malachite and Escapade (son
and daughter) and Blue Cap, a grandson, of that illustrious
sire.
Loyalty has improved to a great extent since he
has been at Flemington, and he will run very forward in
the Cup ; hut he will find Tridentate, Ruenalf and Ronda
hard nuts to crack.
The starting-machine was tried with great
success in the Nursery and Windsor Handicaps, and ’tis
to be hoped that ’twill be adopted for all races in future.
There was a full 15 minutes’ delay in the start for Caulfield
Cup, and the man with the flag was hooted when he
returned to the enclosure.
Gale, by the imported Nautilus, will run a great
colt in the Maribyrnong Plate, and will much trouble
the much-vaunted Challenger. In fact, at starting prices
he will prove a better investment than “Westralia
Wilson’s colt.
The Syndicate pulled back most of their
Chesterman losses over Escapade, who started at a nice
price for Windsor Handieap. Her rider, Luckman, really
seems to be the “lucky” man, or boy, of the season,
and, as he has good hands and head, he should make a
jock, far above the “spur and whalebone" brigade.
Query; How many races are lost yearly through the
inordinate use of the whip?
A Melb. hairdresser offers to “ artistically paint
out black eyes received at football, boxing, Sto.” “ &c.”
is good.
♦ * • •
A Ballarat cabman, to win a 10s. bet, climbed
to the top of the flagpole on the Town Hall tower, and
when a policeman, who had chased him to the roof,
reached the pole, “cabby was standing in a stooping
position on the apex, hanging on with his hands and
feet, waiting for the wind to subside before assuming an
erect attitude. He remained so for three minutes, and
then yielded to the solicitations of the officer and slid
down.” He is to be prosecuted for “ being illegally on
the premises.” That is like prosecuting the man who
enters the lion's cage for trespass.
What became of Tom Coker, of the nose, father
of Melbourne betting ?
• • * ♦
Betting odds are never fair. The fieldsman has
to live, while the punter only blues hig superfluous cash.
• • * •
Dear Bulletin, —I know a stutterer by profes-
sion, who always “ gets even ” with his sporting losses
by “ double or quits.” As the coin is twirling downwards
his impediment goes—“ He-he-he-he —” if it descends,
revealing the “woman” side—“he-he-he-fail/” And
the dickens of it is you can’t swear that he wasn’t trying
to say “ tail " all the time. — Yours, Dipso.

Mr, Albert Sharp, the discoverer of the
new Cannalin remedies for piles, announces that,
in future, there will be a single charge of one
pound for the six weeks course of treatment.
This will avoid delay in serious cases from remote
parts of the colonies, as a pound-note can be sent
where postal-order is not available. Applications
must*be accompanied by a_ full description of the
case, and be addressed plainly to 257 George-st.,
Sydney, N.S.W.
The Waverley Hotel, Oollins-place, Melb.,
has been leased by Miss Henningham, and entirely
refitted and decorated from roof to basement.
Fred. Henningham, so long and favorably known
in connection with the Taylor-Carrington Co., is
at present assisting in the management.*
In the business columns of this issue Mr.
George Crowley, the energetic manager of the
City Mutual Life Office, draws attention to the
remarkable progress his society has made during
the past five years. During its brief career the
City Mutual has secured a good business connec-
tion in the insurance world, and, in the words of
its manager, it “invites the fullest inquiry and
the freest criticism of its figures.’’ This office has
made sundry important innovations in insurance
business and has always maintained a liberal and
progressive system in its dealings with policy
holders.l _ . .

* * * *
The currency of China proves that it is the
devil’s own country. The unprinciples of a prostituted
bi-metallism reign like the worst breed of nightmare. In
the first place, cash or li is cast from an alloy of copper,
iron and tin. One thousand of these should go to the
tael, but they don’t. The number was, perhaps, 1700 last
week, and will be, perhaps, 1600 next week. A tael is a
quantity of silver about the size of a lump of chalk. A
tael in Shanghai is by no means a tael in Haikwan. But
a tael in Shanghai can with reasonable uncertainty be
said to be 1-3937 Mexican dollars. One hundred Haikwan
taels equal 111-40 Shanghai taels = 101‘75 Amoy taels =
106-40 Uhefoo taels=lo4--l6 VVuhu taels=llo Foochow
taels = 113 - 76 Hoihow taels, &c., &o. If you are a China-
man you can enjoy this. But the worst remains to be
told. These taels are either measured in shoes (ingots) of
silver or in a miscellaneous collection of dollars of varying
weight, thus: —
Japanese Trade Doilar=42o grains troy.
Japanese Yen =416 ~ ~
Hongkong Dollar =416 ~ ,’
Mexican do. =4l6£ ~ ~’
Carolus =414 „ „
On top of all this you have to pile the final complication
that each district has its conventional discount and pre-
mium. In some places you have to give so much silver
over the nominal amount, otherwheres you get a discount
you didn’t expect.
The queerest thing in currency is a dollar that
lias gone the rounds of China. Every merchant who gets
hold of the coin puts his chop , shro/mark, or monogram
on it, with a brass stamp and a hammer. The conse-
quence is chaos.
* » * *
The silver exports from London to the East
were about normal in the first eight months of '94, if we
reckon the gross amount at the sterling value. There is,
of course, an enormous advantage to this year over last)
if we reckon the weight shipped. The following are the
figures for eight months ;
1893. 1894.
, India .. .. £4,939,452 £3,705,715
China .. 834,013 2,040,873
Straits .. 960,340 877,652
£6,733,805 £6,624,240
It will be seen that China’s needs have been hugely
increased this year, almost atoning for the brake that has
been put on Indian importation by the cesahtym of
coinage.

Dear Bulletin, —Your reference to the bill re-
forming the law re mortgages, introduced by J. M. Cross,
in Q. Assembly, recalls to me an idea, suggested by a per-
sonal knowledge of the subject, that a further alteration
might well be made in the interest of laborers employed
on mortgaged property, on agricultural land in particular.
In this country the farmer is dependent solely on the pro-
ceeds of his harvest for the means to pay the men whom
he has to prepare the ground for the crop, to
sow and to reap it. The men know this, and are content
to wait for their wages until the crop is sold. In marry
cases they work for nearly a year clearing, gruhbinv
ploughing, &c.—getting little besides their tucker-in the
expectation of being paid in full when the product of
tneir toil has been disposed of.
Now, take the case of a farmer whose land is
under mortgage. He owes money to his storekeeper, his
laborers and others, all waiting for harvest-time. Before
that arrives, however, the mortgagee takes possession,
sells him off, and, as is usually the case, the amount
realised is scarcely sufficient to repay the mortgage debt,
t here is then no money left to pay the wages of the men
"i , 08 ® *' ol ‘ nas actually improved and made more market-
able the security, and the mortgagee gets the benefit of this
increased value for nothing! The above is not a supposi-
tion, as I know of many instances where the wiong has
been done. It may be said that the laborer has a remedy
against his employer; but it is a useless one. Where is the
good of obtaining a judgment when one cannot get the
rruits o i ! The employers’ personal property is all
under bill-of-sale or stock mortgage, and at the
least sign of danger the holders of these instruments
exercise their legal rights and sell for what they
can get The Bankruptcy Act makes a preferential
debt of laborers wages, not exceeding £5O, for ser-
yices rendered to the bankrupt during six montl s
before the date of the bankruptcy order ; but where is
thejse of proving a debt when the estate is worth
nothcvg owing to the “ secured ” creditors having pounced
on all the assets? Consequently, the one who has been
producing the means to pay the interest due to the mort-
gagee of i,he land, and who has also been increasing th ;
value of the security upon which the principal was
advanced, js compelled to whistle for his waves. The
lienee or hmder of a, crop lien, is compelled, in the event
of his taking possession of the crop, to pay any rent due
to the landlord by the tenant whose crop is seized, or the
interest due to the mortgagee if the land is mort>'av e d.
Truly, property has some “sacred ” rights !
j mortgagee* on taking possession, were
compelled to pay ;fce wages ,->f the men who had been
employed on the land for, Say, *2 months prior to the
date of seizure, a great injustice to a class too little able
to protect themselves would he prevented. Surely this is
a matter that is deserving of attention from those of onr
legislators who believe that “Property” has had in the
past too much protection, and flesh and blood too little.
—D.D.k.
* * * *
Geologist Jack, of Queensland, has gone West
to inspect the artesian strata. He enjoys this purely
scientific visit as a break from his ordinary duties which
he quaintly says are popularly expected to be those of “ a
quasi mining-tipster.” The public demand too much of
geologists, and are ever ready to assail them when pay.
able results are obtained from any ground ori"inaliy
evoking unfavourable reports from the scientist He
cannot predict, but merely deduces. He endorses the
opinion of the miner who says, “ Where it is-there it is!”
In the old Cornish vernacular—
“ Wha’ it be,
Tha’ it be,
And wha’ it be,
Tha' baint Oi !”
* * * »
A colossal pile awaits the man who locates the
big cement deposit which is the source of the great
Noitheru alluvial gold-lead (Park's, N.S.W.). Twenty
per cent, of the “ wash ” in this lead is cement, and this
cement averages as much gold as the dirt, and has, there-
fore, to be crushed. The cement deposit is believed to be
within a mile or two of the lead. Here is a chance for the
diamond drill.

summer in Europe, and consequently large quan-
tities’Of the season’s goods were left on hand, and

Manufacturing districts.
And, CASH IN HAND, had the pick of the
most beautiful of the goods made for the European
summer trade. This splendid operation will have

in natural course upon the selling prices. The
cost of a dress will be less, while the assurance
will remain that the gown exhibits every fashion-
able characteristic. So marked is the difference

Br PRICE WARUNG.
Premier Reid is evidently thinking about
taking off the N.S.W. sugar-duty. This means
that the N.S.W. sugar-industry, which supports,
directly and indirectly, a good many thousand
workers and their families, must close up, or else
that wages must come down to the Kanaka
rates paid in Queensland. Up North now the
white cane-cutter; working;by contract, can, if he
toils from sunrise to sunset, earn about 3s. a day,
out of which he has to find his own food. This is
what must come in N.S. Wales if Reid acts up to
his convictions. And if he doesn’t act up to his
convictions, for what earthly purpose is he in office
as a 1 reetrade Premier ?
When John Downer, Q.C., was S.A.
Premier recently, his Govt, got notice from the
official draftsman that there was a flaw in the
rules made by the Supreme Court Judges under
u 6 P ro * J ; l * ;e Act of ’92. No notice was taken of
the warning, but after the Cabinet resigned, John
Downer, Q.C., as solicitor in the Price Maurice
estate, brought arguments to bear on the Supreme
Court which resulted in the rules being pro-
nounced ultra vires. During the two years, about
XoOOO was collected in fefes, and the Kingston
Govt, recently brought in a Bill to validate the
rules, but this _ has been bitterly contested by
Downer Q.C., in his capacity as leader of the
Opposition, with the result that its retrospective
powers have been excised. It is only fair to Downer
| to say that he solemnly avers that his Attorney-
General (Homburg) did not show him the com-
munication sent in by the draftsman in October of
last year; but the coincidence is a somewhat awk-
ward one for the Q.C.
Miss M. Fleming, late of Melb. Reform
Club Hotel, announces that she has taken the
Hotel Metropolis in Bourke-street, one of the
handsomest hotels in the Southern city.
The Esplanade Hotel, at St. Kilda, still
retains its place as one of the few elegant and
fashionable Victorian hotels. In point of situa-
tion it is positively unrivalled, commanding the
best possible view of Hobson’s Bay and Port
Phillip. Trams pass the door every ten minutes,
and the city may easily be reached within a
quarter of an hour. Mr. ;ff. Jacoby is still the
popular host of this favorite establishment.
Sydney Wool Sales.
M'Burnev, Wallis & Co. report: —At the sales in the
early part of this week, competition for good greasy wool
was slightly better than at last auctions, although no
actual improvement in prices may be quoted. Scoured
wool is, if any alteration, slightly under opening rates. I
The outlook of the m irket is not encouraging, and we see !
no reason for anticipating much increase in prices. The
wool lo hand so far shows increased weight as compared
with last season, which was also a heavier year than its
predecessor. We, would again warn owners against
hampering the sale of their wool by placing excessive
reserves upon it. The judgment of his auctioneer, who
has all descriptions of wool daily under his notice, must i
be the best guide a producer can have. To-day we are
offering an attractive catalogue.
Sydney, Oct. 19, 1891.
A FEW FACTS WORTH NOTINU.
During the past five years The City Mutual
Life Assurance Society has made greater pro-
portionate strides than any other Life office
doing business in Australia. It alone has
shewn a continuous increase in the volume of
new business transacted--always the test of
the popularity of an institution. At the
beginning of 1889 its funds barely totalled
£40,000, now the annual income and invested
funds exceed £160,000 stg. This increasing
popularity has been earned solely on its own
claims to public favor—never on the demerits,
manufactured or otherwise--of any other
institution. Now and again the City Mutual
has had to complain of unfair rivalry and
has lately been reluctantly compelled to
drop a hint that this sort of nonsense had
better stop once and for ever.
But at the same time it invites criticism--
the fullest inquiry and the freest critioism--
of its figures. The Sydney Bulletin--
nothing if not plainspoken--recently com-
mented om the somewhat heavy expense sheet
last year. Every assistance was thereupon
accorded the Bulletin to compare the expenses
of other first-class offices at a similar
age and to note the volume of new business
transacted by the City Mutual as compared
with that of every other Australian Life
Office. The Bulletin, searching for truth
and finding it, puts the case of the City
Mutual fairly before the assuiing public in
its issue of the 29th ult.
The Balance Sheets and Actuarial Reports
of this Society--the people's office--are
always open for the perusal and study of
everybody--intending assurer or otherwise--
and it may be of interest to learn in con-
nection therewith that the City Mutual is
the only office in Australia which periodi-
cally submits its figures to the examination
of a professional actuary in no way con-
nected with the Society.
Send for prospectus and table of rates.
Head Office: intersection Hunter, Bligh and
Castlereagh streets, Sydney.
GEO. CROWLEY,
Manager.

Max O’Rell on Australia .
Great fleas have little fleas upon their backs to
bite ’em ; and Australia, for her sins, has Max
O’Rell. Max is, indeed, a literary flea, hopping
round the world with Gallic agility, and generally
drawing blood wherever he goes. The
not a disagreeable one. To travel from country
to country with open eyes and ears stretched to
catch club gossip and society doubles-entendres;
to spy out the nakedness of your hosts in their
unguarded moments as an usher spies behind the
dormitory door, exulting over the rod he has in
pickle (though it cannot be too often affirmed that
Max was never an usher, always a Professor);
and then to dish up your impressions with
sauce piquante in a nice little book which
your victims buy while howling—is not this
a delightful jin-de-sihde occupation ? But not
everybody has Max’s talent to live on the
enemy, and to put them so dexterously in the
position of Byron’s stricken eagle. It is no dis-
praise to say that Max borrows an epigram here
ft nd steals a chestnut there, and that his books
are merely an olla-podrida of unacknowledged
thieveries. Eor he still has the merit of showing
now it is done, and no one grumbles because
Oolumbus did not create America, as well as dis-
cover it. Granted that Max is but a concocter of
an exhausted diner frequently prefers
a French cook’s rechauffe to the most splendid
jirloin; and Max O’Rell hashes and combines
and garnishes with all the deftness of a Soyer.
One’s first impression about “John Bull and
Jo.” is that it is very inaccurate and very
Jull. A second impression is that it is rather
nore inaccurate and a little duller. But that is
iot Max’s fault. The diary detail appears dreary
oecause it is taken from our own diary ; the jokes
je em cheap and ohestnutty because they are our
jwn old jokes. Only familiarity breeds contempt
for Max’s wit. Consider what a sensation will be
jaused in England by the story about the woman
jvho wouldn't take a lift on the train because she
was in a hurry ! How the new gloomounsts will
writhe in impotent jealousy of the man who can
sparkle like that I And when Max goes on to say
that when he saw an Australian train passing he
•nvoluntarily lifted his hat, is it possible to doubt
that old-world readers will roll on the ground in
scstasy ? After all, nothing could be fairer. Aus-
tr Bans have laughed at John Bull for hanging
“Thou God seest me!” in his bedroom; they
have laughed at friend Macdonald for debiting
his son with holiday ham-and-eggs; why should
not Macdonald and Bull, in turn, enjoy them-
selves at the expense of Australian railways?
Why not, indeed ?
• * *
If one can divest one’s-self of Australian pre-
judices, and attack “John Bull and Co.” with a
clean memory and a right spirit, there is a good
deal to smile over and a little to think over It is
abundantly evident, indeed, that Max O Rell hf.i
little critical insight. He has never been able to
separate wheat from chaff in all the stories which
have been told him, .and the views which he
voices are simply those of the people he has mixed
with _ unsifted, unanalysed, uncorroborated.
1 has his book cannot be taken seriously.
It is full of half - truths which leave a
wholly wrong impression; of facts set forth
without the reservations and conditions
which alter their character completely; of dis-
torted glimpses, disproportionate estimates, and
misunderstood meanings. The saddest mistake of
all is the portrait of Parkcs, whose mane has been
clipped and combed by a Delilah-like artist until
all his glory has departed. The next saddest
thing is the hoax played on Max by a Sydney
clubman, “one of the foremost merchants of the
town,” who told Max he couldn’t go to the lec-
tures because he was drunk every evening from
seven o’clock. “ Drunkenness of that description
is so repulsive to me that I forthwith left the
dining-rqom,” says the shocked Max.
# * *
Unluckily, Max took the joke as a bit of typical
reality, and he cannonades the drink-curse
throughout a whole chapter—invariably citing
extreme cases as examples of national character.
What logical reputation would a traveller preserve
in France who held up a Parisian absinthe-wreck
as showing the spiritual degradation of the French
nation? Undoubtedly too much alcohol is con-
sumed in Australia, and the daily practice of
drinkingspiritsis viciousand unhealthy. Drunken-
ness is our worst legacy from our Teutonic
ancestors. But one questions whether its results
are shown to worse advantage in Australia than
in England, for example. And Max O’Rell
omitted to note the undoubted temperance of
native Australians, who as a class very rarely
drink to excess. Nor does it help to say that—
It is not uncommon in Australia to see a young man
arrive in a town, hand over a cheque for fifty or sixty
pounds to the keeper of some hotel, saying to him, “Let
me have as much drink as I want. When I have drunk
my cheque, let me know, and I will go home.”
That pre-historio knocker-down of cheques is
deader than Captain Cook—killed by “depression”
and the march of civilisation. And he never was
a young man, anyhow.
Away from the Curse, Max opines to better
advantage. He ridicules with justice the cant
and snobbery prevailing among the “ right-think-
ing” classes of Australia, and his parody on Sum-
mary Smith’s prayer that God would restore con-
fidence in the reconstructed banks is as apt as it
is eloquent. He has found time, too, to notice
that “missionary boys” join the faults of their
old state with the vices of their new religion. Of
the Zulus, for instance
In the natural state, the Zulus are honest and their
women are virtuous. When they have gone through the
apprenticeship of civilisation in the missions, the womens
virtue often loses much of its rigidity, and the men lie
and cheat like “Christians” of the deepest dye. The
Zulus are virtuous and honest by instinct , and it is diffi-
cult to see how their childlike souls can be improved by a
theorv which, after all, may be summed up in these few
words • “ Do not sin, but if you sin, make yourself easy,
you have only to believe and all your sins will be blotted
out ” “ Let us sin, then,” say the converted Zulus, too
often ; “ the more we sin, the more will be forgiven us.
• * *
So on the whole, Max is a disappointment. He
is getting successful and lazy now, and will not
take pains to sharpen up his bright sayings ; nor
does he trouble to make sure of-their justice being
content to father any haphazard opinion that he
comes across. His mistakes are no matter; but
when one thinks of the splendid field of Australian
humour yet unreaped by the book-makers, it is a
grievance that he should not glean a better harvest,
considering his repute. But, after all. Max
O’Rell is rather an author of reputation than ot
repute. “ That makes a difference, don tit ?
Dear Bulletin, —How is this for a tight
place? Many years ago I deserted from an Eng-
lish ship on which I was an apprentice, and shipped
in the little topsail schooner Hesperus, of Auck-
land, bound to Tahiti. On a dark night wo ran
into a coral reef 20 miles from Papeete ; the vessel
was entirely broken up in the surf, and out of a
crew of seven only the mate (who, by the way,
was a baronet) and myself were saved. We lay
on the beach all night, unable to walk, scratched
and torn by the jagged coral, and were found next
morning at daylight by some native women
bathers. They ran off, but soon returned accom-
panied by the inhabitants of the whole village,
and, after a consultation, three or four of the young
men were sent away and came back with bundles
of green leaves, which were distributed to the
others and which they all immediately began to
chew, sitting in a circle meanwhile around us.
Each mouthful after mastication was placed in
front of the chevver, and all the time this was
going on I thought the fodder was to be used as
stuffing, and that we were to be seasoned and
eaten. Of course I was mistaken, but I shall
never forget the terrible quarter of an hour spent
in watching the preparation of the condiment
that was to make my baked clay taste nice. They
spread the stuff they had prepared on our outs
and bruises, and so effectual was it that we could
actually feel the pain being drawn from us- in
great throbs. I am a canvasser now, and if I
could get some of that ointment I would patent a
“ heal-all ” salve that would make my fortune. —
O.E.H. [What is to be the name of your oint-
ment ? Bulletin rates, 7s. 6d. per inch.—Ed. B.J

“ T hold the Crown of Germany from God
alone, and it is my conviction that I am guided by
the Almighty in all my actions.”— William, of
Yarrnany.
Which shows it takes a head that’s level
To know your God now from your Devil.
AND THIS IS ONE OF THEM.
There are some truths that ought to be blown
through a million speaking trumpets every hour;
that ought to be printed in big type on the front
page of every newspaper ; that ought to be painted
on every signboard at the crossroads ; that ought
to be taught in every school. And this is one of
them : There is no such thing on earth as a tonic
medicine.
People talk of “tonics” and doctors talk of
y tonics.” Pshaw ! When a country is discovered
in which no food stuffs are ever raised orimported,
and in which the men, women, and children are
all well and hearty, we may conclude they subsist
on some sort of “tonic.” The mischief this
delusion about tonics has done is beyond calcula-
tion. It leads the sick to lean on broken reeds,
to expect relief from a source from whicli it is
simply impossible that relief can come, to waste
money in buying “tonics,” and precious time in
waiting for these alleged “ tonics ” to work
miracles.
Mr. Thomas Foster, of 15, Chatham Place,
Adelaide Street. Hull, in an account of a recent
illness, says, among other things, this: “ I then
tried stomach tonics, but they did me no good.”
Suppose we have his whole story, which is short,
and make our comment on it afterwards. He says:
“ Up to the month of June, 1891, I was strong and
healthy. At that time I fell into a low, weak
condition. I felt languid and heavy, and was
always tired. I had a foul taste in the mouth,
and a dreadful pain in the chest and sides after
eating, whilst my stomach was like a burning fire.
I was much troubled with wind, which seemed to
roll all over me, and I had a constant belching and
rising in my throat. I was in agony day and
night, and for hours I walked about the room
rubbing my chest in the effort to obtain relief.
I lost a deal of sleep, and felt worse tired in the
morning than when I went to bed. Gradually I
became weaker and weaker until I had hard work
to follow my employment, for I was in misery all
the time.
“ I went to a doctor, who sounded me and gave
me medicines, but I got no relief, and after taking
his tnedieines for a month, I left off going to him.
I then tried stomach tonics and other medicines but
nothing did me any good. In this state I con-
tinued week after week, growing more feeble all
the while. I felt that if I did not soon find a
remedy I should be done for altogether.
“In October, 1891, a book was left at my house
telling of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup, and describing a case like mine that
had been cured by it. As I had often heard this
medicine well spoken of, I made up my mind to
try it, and got a bottle from Mr. Cousins, in
Anlaby Road. After I had taken two doses, I felt
grateful relief, and before I had quite finished the
bottle I was completely cured, and have since been
in the best of health.
“ I thank God that this medicine was ever made
known to me. Otherwise I should have been in my
grave before now. I will answer anyone who may
write concerning the facts here set forth. Yours
truly (Signed), Thomas Foster, 15, Chatham Place,
Adelaide Street, Hull, March 24th, 1892.”
Now let us see. The symptoms of Mr. Foster’s
complaint are easily recognisable. He suffered
from indigestion and dyspepsia. The medicines
administered by his physician or purchased by
himself proved useless because they were not
addressed to the disease with which he was actually
afflicted, but possibly to one or more of its symp-
toms. To abolish any existing evil it is always
Causes we must work at —never mere conse-
quences.
- The “ stomach tonics ” which Mr. Foster hoped
might relieve him may have done so for a moment
on precisely the same principle that a sharp
application of whip and spur wakes up a tired
horse, not by giving him strength, but by rousing
his reserved nervous force, with a deeper reaction
to follow. That’s how it ever was and will be.
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup restored this
gentleman to real and genuine health by cleansing
his system of the poison of disease, by removing
the obstacles and enabling the stomach to retain
and digest food. A very simple thing, yet how
hard to accomplish, oh ! my masters.
The remedy does it, however, as is testified by
a host of witnesses all over the world —witnesses
who say more in praise of its merits than you
would have time to read.

lection of choice classics from a book-shop, should

S.M. Herald asks if the cry of “Australia
for Australians ” be anything but an empty sound,
why is it that we beg in England and Ireland
for the support of our churches. First, who are
they who raise the cry ? and second, who are they
who so beg? The unification of these two parties
is wildly impossible.

pitals are “hard-cases.” Lately, in a N.S.W"

A Kumara (M.L.) paper tells a double fat-
pig yarn about two of the fattest Berkshire one-
year-olds ever killed. They were so fat that they
had long since ceased eating their food standing,
and adipose tissue had grown over their eyes,
making them stone-blind. When hung up they
looked more like square boxes than pigs.
“A Golden Shanty,” by Bulletin writers,
is one of the most striking products of nascent
Australian literature. Its contents are gems of
native lustre, glowing with no borrowed brilliancy.
Their breezy humour, their weird pathos, spring
alike from Southern soil, under a Southern sky,
and their promise is sui generis, unique and
unapproachable. The second edition now ready ;
post-free to any Australasian address, Is. 3d. ;
foreign, Is. fid.
The Argus creed for Victoria throughout
the elections was :
1. Reduce high duties.
2. Reduce extravagant expenditure.
3. Reduce cost of government.
i. For any new revenue tax wealth in all its forms.
Then the elections came, and Patterson and the
Argus were routed. The day after Parliament
re-gathered the Argus attempted a strategic
retreat. The creed for the country, it said, advo-
cated by us and adopted by the country, is as
follows: —
1. Direct taxation.
2. Reduced cost of government.
3. Tariff reform.
Here we see the right-about. “ Tax wealth in all
its forms ” was the last resort in the old creed, but
now it is No. 1. There must be some real political
worth in Victoria when the Argus trims like this.
A Melb. lady lately returned home after a
brief absence and found the front door open, most
of the portable goods packed up for removal, and
a gentlemanly-looking person on the premises.
As she entered he took off his glossy hat and ex-
plained matters. He had been passing by, it
seemed, and detecting signs of burglary he entered
and engaged in a desperate conflict with a
rough-looking malefactor, whom he at length
routed with great slaughter. Then he stood
guard over the property until the return of
the rightful owner, and was pleased to say,
&c. Here he took off his hat again, and the
flurried lady suddenly noticed, with a shriek of
dismay, that it was her husband’s hat, and that
he had her husband’s clothes on, and that her
husband’s watch-chain was hanging out of his
pocket, whereupon the gentlemanly person sprinted
away down the street and vanished. He was cap-
tured later on. All of which goes to show that
politeness is not always profitable. If he hadn’t
taken off the borrowed hat so effusively, the
chances are that no special attention would have
been drawn to it and the rest of his borrowed
plumes.

Canvassers reckon that, granted lOmin. in-
terview with a prospective buyer, the sale of an
article is practically complete. The trouble is to
get 10 minutes’ talk. People, as a rule, don’t give
the canvasser a chance to properly introduce him-
self. The door opens an inch or two, in answer
to his gentle, insinuating tap, and before he has
time to speak somebody snaps, “Nothing to day.’
Then bang goes the door! This device has hither-
to been to the householder what a similar bang-
ing of the door on the “retreating form” of an
“awakening woman ” is to Ibsen and the up-to-
date novel-writers on social questions—it saves a
deal of explanation and trouble. But the wily
fin-de-sUcle canvasser, when every respectable un-
employed is of the genus, has invented a method
of preventing this ill-treatment of the innocent
door. He fixes upon a house, trips gaily up the
front steps, and gives one great tug at the bell-
pull and lets go. Ere the jangling of the bell
ceases, an infuriated Somebody is there to in-
vestigate the meaning of the violence. Just
what the canvasser wants, for the door doesn’r
bang during these enquiries 1 With a peace-be-to
this-house kind of smile on a slightly-abashed
countenance, and raising his hat politely, he pro-
ceeds to explain, amid profuse apologies, that the
bell-pull was stiff and jerky, that he slipped and
clutched wildly at it to avert a fall—anything, in
short, to prevent the banging of the door. Then
gradually and skilfully the apologies glide, almost
imperceptibly, into an all-round introduction of
any article he may have on sale. People like
being apologised to—it gives them a good opinion
of themselves—and the mollified housewife who
came in a violent hurry to sool the canvasser oil
the premises, and to bang the door, generally
remains to buy. What is wanted now is for
someone to invent means for stopping the eternul
door-banging in latter-day fiction, when the
heroine awakens to her mission, and goes out into
the cruel world, never, never to return, leaving
her husband to tend the baby.

Tales of the Riverine.
THE SNAGGING OF THE BAUCY NELL.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Half-speed astern ! Easy now, for rd!
Stand ready wi’ the fenders ! Slow ’em down !
What’s her depth, Fred ? ’Stern there—sling off
the barge-rope ! Gently, now, gently ! D !
that lubbe«mit ’ud make a parson swear ! Easy,
now, easy ! Fend her off that timber, Johnson !”
So, with breathless exclamations in a high,
harsh naonotong, Skipper Cane whipped his men
into methodic, intelligent action, and brought the
Saucy Nell and her barge carefully against the
hillock on which stood the slaughtering gallows of
Caloonaga homestead. .
Going up stream, ten days back, to Menmdie,
the Nell had passed that hillock and homestead
half s a-mfle to the westward. The river was then
falling, and at places she had to throw out
warps and tackling and haul up over the bed
ridges and half-emerging snags. Now, the trunks
to which her warps had been fastened were them-
selves half-a-mile to the westward of her present
position; and her paddles beat the surface of a
plain of water between 30 and 40 feet above the
level of that they skimmed up voyage, when the
Nell had scraped with her bottom planks the roots
which great gums on the river-margin had thrust
treacherously out into the river.
Within that short period Nature had burst into
one of those grim, ironic moods with which she
occasionally tempers her tyranny over the Far
West of the Riverine —as though Peter the Cruel
sought his recreation in wearing the mask of Vol-
taire, or Torquemada that of the mocking Heine.
* * *
Nature had held the West in the red-hot
bonds of drought long enough to drive many a
brave pioneering soul into the sweating-rooms of
the banks and cognate purgatories, and now she
answered the universal prayer for rain by sending
a deluge that was only less destructive than the
drought. “Up north,” the middle country, and
the southern areas which ran up to the Murray—
all had shared in the derisive outpouring of the
sky’s fountains ; and already life that had hardly
survived the strangling heat was fighting with
might and main the throttling waters. Man,
cattle, and sheep, the feathered things and the
creeping things, cowered beneath the storm as they
had shrivelled beneath the scorching sun. The rain-
gaugers grew neglectful of the erstwhile precious
“ points,” and affected contempt for anything less
than “inches and odds were offered more freely
than they were taken as to the likelihood of the
flood-marks of ’64 being easily surpassed. “ Sixty-
four” was the standard year of terror on the
Darling till this season —which was to hold the
prize of wretchedness till the fateful ’9O.
For ’64 had not known the desolation that this
year of the late seventies was to know.
Settlement was sparse and intermittent then;
now mammoth rUJM jostled one another and shared
boundary-fences Then, the selector was repre-
sented by a dummy hut per six-forty-acre block.
Now, the staunch souls who had “ peacocked,” to
the gnashing of squatters’ teeth, and who declined
to be bought out, were by no means rare.
With the people, the people’s homes were chang-
ing to a different order of architecture than the
primeval slab-hut and bare, rude, “w.b.” home
stead. And stock had multiplied and increased
in the land tenfold. So, what with one thing and
another, the devils of drought and flood reaped
a rich harvest of ruin. The glory of success
and the ideals of enjoyment which dull, prosaic
labour, continued for years, had brought within
reach of the Far West settlers, was thrust just
beyond gra>sp by the thought. Then came the
swirling, hurling rains and washed all dreams of
success, all possibilities of pleasure, almost all
realities of achievement, still farther away, till
feeble hearts drowned, and great ones drooped.
The hell of the heat, and the hell of the waters—
between them what hope of the paradise which is
created by a full purse?
* * *
Fairfax’s Caloonaga station had been un-
fortunate, though not singularly so. Its 40,000
sheep had been reduced, by three years of grass-
lessness, to 25,000. The lambings had been lamb-
ings of a percentage so paltry that “ the cove ”
was ashamed to send in the usual par. to the
Pastobal Times. The rivers had been “ down
and, consequently, what poor clip of wool there
was had either been carted to Wentworth at
enormous expense, or else was still in the shed
waiting transit. When he negotiated the pur-
chase the agent through whom he bought had
demonstrated, on paper, with facile lip and pen,
the magnificent results that would be realised in
these magic three years.
“ Wool ?” Oh, that would never be lower than
a shilling a pound in the grease! “Stores?”
Bless you—dirt cheap! “Fats?” —the native
grasses on Caloonaga were almost as succulent as
the Terinallum, or Werronggurt, English-grassed
pastures in famous Western Vic., and it only cost
a trifle to drive “fats” to the great Victorian
markets ! “ Removal of clip—carriage of stores ?”
Why, Providence had specially designed Caloonaga
to be a fortune-breeding run, and did not the
Darling flow with its broad, navigable stream
within half-a-mile of the station and homestead,
thus affording the most remarkable facilities!
“ Selectors ?” Devil a one of them on the station
except those who had dummied on its behalf !
Site of homestead, freehold—all best spots of run,
if there could be any best where all was good—
also dummied in interests of owners ! “ Why was
this desirable and profitable property in the
market, then?” Oh, vendor made his pile, going
“ home ” to spend it —wouldn’t sell now if he had
any intention of coming back again, but old folks
at home, y’ know —fond of ’em—go back, and play
part of Lord Bountiful, y’ know !
Thus, the agent—on paper, and with most plau-
sible and most eloquent of voices; and'Fairfax
drew a cheque for twenty thousand pounds, signed
bills for fifty thousand more, and—forgot to
obtain a guarantee against droughts and floods.
And so, when the Saucy Nell pushed her nose
over what, two days before, had been a heat-
cursed plain, and clutched with one of her grap-
nels the crossbar of the butcher’s gallows, he had
no heart to enjoy the, novelty of the spectacle.
The up-springing of the waters had nearly com-
pleted, he thought, what the dry term had begun.
With twenty-five thousand sheep, his wool at
Echuca, and a fair season, he might have saved
foreclosure at least. With ten thousand rotting
carcases, floated by the swollen waters into the tops
of scrub and mallee and the branches of red-gum
and blackwood, the dismal watery waste before
him was not the dismalest of prospects that his
eye beheld at intervals. He caught a glimpse also
of the desert of bankruptcy, with never an oasis
of hope therein. He knew he was not alone in his
plight, but whenever did another’s pangs bring
true healing to one’s own wounds ?
Fairfax had been but a few years out
from England, and had not, therefore, like so
many other Ri veiiue squatters, been made tanu lar
with vicissitudes by an experience of the gold-
diggings. Men who had known the rough-and-
tumble of existence on the Turon, Forest Free <,
the VVaranga, and Ballarat; who had been one day
as paupers and on the morrow as princes; who had
borne the stress of long despair, and the more
intense strain of a plunge into sudden wealth; who
had flung away aimlessly in thriftless profusion the
gold which was never to come again to them ; men
of these experiences would have taken another
“ facer ” from fortune with something of Spartan
philosophy. They would have given over the
station to the mortgagees agent without fuss, and
have stepped out valiantly on the wallaby—conn-
dent that if the dice fell against them this tune
they might, fall toward them at another.
Fairfax was not of such tempered and tried
stuff. His twenty thousand had been in-
herited ; and his nature knew not the
strengthening which goes with the accumula-
tion of gold, when the gold is won by the
honest work of the man himself and is not coined
out of the heart-blood of others. He was, to tell
the truth, but a poor creature as yet. What
adversity would make of him has to be seen.
He welcomed the Saucy Nell’s skipper with a
feeble smile and speech of feebler humor as he
brought the boats across the low_ banks which
separated and would separate again the station
buildings from the river. .
“ Well, Captain, you’ve scarcely to complain of
knocking up your hands warping now ! ”
“No, Mr. Fairfax—that’s not our trouble now.
It never rains but it pours out here, and, by Jove,
we’ve got the pouring to rights ! Forty feet above
summer level already, and another ten feet coming
—what with the Paroo and the Queensland back
waters! Tnere’s going to be a h of a time,
sir ! ” , , ,
Fairfax started at the news, and abandoned any
resolve he might have had to sustain his poor
attempt at jocularity. Another ten feet would
overwhelm all his as yet unsubmerged ground,
and then—good-bye to the remnants of his flocks
which were too weak to travel up country ! With
the vividly-pessimistic imagination of the spine-
less man, he, in the next moment, saw the water
rise and spread, and keep on spreading, encroach-
ing upon the phantom of pasturage left him,
drowning it and the paltry thousands of stupid
sheep; he heard it lapping against the sagging
boards of his woolshed, where the ninety-odd bales
which represented his past year’s clip were await-
ing removal; he felt it sapping his homestead
and his future. Like all weak men, he ex-
aggerated the possibilities ; and to a weak man
his fantastic terrors are very real. To lairfax
it was as though, in very deed, the advancing
edge of the water bore ruin in its darkest form
insidiously upon him.
“ Another rise ! God !’’ he muttered, dazedly.
Skipper Cane stared at him, with something of
contempt in his look. Cane himself had been
clewed up once or twice by dry spells, and he made
fresh starts again and again. Hundreds of others
had done the same. What the dickens, then,
should the boss of Caloonaga be so fearful about.
“ Well, Mr. Fairfax—steady, there, you chaps
with them poles his men were poling the barge
into what had been a “ dead finish ” scrub before
the flood came down—“you’ll have her ripped up
’fore you know where you are—beg pardon, Mr.
Fairfax, but those d looneys don’t think of
five minutes ahead, and I was just going to say
you think too far ahead. You’re given t“
veck’ning the loss on the insurance before you
snagged the craft! Ain’t you now, sir t
The escaping steam was hissing loudly, ami
perhaps overcame the skipper’s voice, or perhaps
the squatter did not appreciate the river metaphor.
No one “ever counted the chickens before they
were hatched ”on the rivers—you always “ drank
the village whisky before the grog was shipped
Neither did any one “meet trouble half-way.
It was always “ counting the loss on the insurance
before the boat was snagged.” But Fairfax was
in no mood to apply the metaphor if he understood
it. He took it literally. . , , ,
“ What do you mean, Captain ? he questioned
nervously. “Do you think there is any danger ot
snagging ? What’ll become of my wool ?”
“I see, Mr. Fairfax,” laughed the skipper,
“ you don’t understand me ! I meant as you re a
little bit too hasty in counting the worst of things.
Things never kern out so bad as we suspect-that s
my motter, sir.” ,_ . , . „
“Oh, hang philosophy!” rejoined Fairfax, lull
of the thought suggested by Cane’s previous
words. “Is there really any special danger oi
your snagging this trip ?”
“ Well, there is—and there’s no use a-denymg
it!”
“ Good God !” . .
“ Oh, I don’t say as I’d grow funky on it, sir,
if I were you .”
“Funky ! —Can’t you understand, man, that
that .” Here he held his speech. He was
becoming foolishly expansive—he reflected. Wby
should he take this rough boatman into his confi-
dence? Why should he tell him how much the
safety of the down cargo meant to him ! He fell
back into the reticence of his class. A fatal reti-
cence, perhaps, as it proved.
• * *
If he had expressed to Cane his fear as to
the danger of the down-trip to Echuca, and his
sense of the tremendous importance to him of the
issues dependent upon it, would the result have
been different ? It is doubtful. When the rivers
were up flood-high, Randell himself, that Columbua
of the Riverine, could not be sure of carrying
himself and his crafts safe into port. You lost
your bearings so easily ! Where was that clump
of mallee that fringed the bluff where the great
Aua branch debouched ? Where were the giant
gums that were ghastly in their unsheathed white-
ness even when the radiance of sunlit skies
shimmered and sheened in the lagoons and billa-
bongs? Where was Wood-Pile Point, which was
the signal of danger hard by the shifting and
treacherous sand-spit that was wont to cuddle
snags till the water above it was stirred by
paddles, and would then jerk up the jagged sides
of felon trees into the planking ? Where were the
blacks’ gunyahs which betokened the approach to
Sturt’s billabong, and warned the steersman to
ware the tangental force of the current at the
bend ? Where the patch of rat-tail grass on open
country by Poencarra, thedelight of the rouseabouts
eyes, because it meant to them a long reach whereon
would be fair running, and, therefore, a time of
loafing and no bothersome sounding. Where were
these and the thousand other guiding-marks?
Under the greenish-blue water, all of them, save
the gums. Those only you saw, and only
their crestc. even then. All the rest were
submerged, and steering was a nightmare.
You poled into good water, and the next instant
your bottoms were crushing through the tops of
mulga. You zig-zagged timorously with the
steamer into a clear channel, and her barge, with


Soap .Makers
By Special Appointment
TO
HER MAJESTY
The Queen
AND
HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
ince or Wales.
a shade less beam than the boat had at the paddle-
boxes, jostled and reeled against some floating
timber that you had slipped over. The dark patch
yonder—was it the bluff at the wood-pile or the
island twenty miles beyond the woodpile? Had
you got back into the natural stream, or were you
in one of Fitzwilliam Wentworth’s stockyards?
Where were you, or, rather, where weren’t you?
The Lord only knew !
Now, with Fairfax’s small clip aboard, topping
up the two hundred which were all, mindful of
the state of the rivers, he had ventured to take
from Menindie, skipper Oane had a light load
enough, and had the streams been certain he
would have skimmed to the junction landslip and
thence to Echuca at racing speed. But the waters
were, as we say, the reverse of certain. The only
certainty about them was that the channel he
thought he was in was distinctly some other
channek
The waters had wiped out the map, and the
nverman’s special knowledge. They amazed him,
knotted his perceptions confusingly, made him
stupid.
* « *
When an ordinarily intelligent man
becomes stupid he also becomes desperate. He is
beaten by the bowelless gods of Fact; and if he
does not take to drink he attempts to ride rough-
shod over things as they are with the impulsive-
ness of an unreasoning animalism. Cane’s care-
fulness being plagued out of him, he literally “let
the boats rip.” If they went over, all right ; if
they didn’t, well, they wouldn’t, and that was the
end of it. He knew the Menindie wool was
insured—he himself was pretty well “covered”
—and if Fairfax was not protected by an “open
policy,” well (again) that was Fairfax’s look-out.
No one but a fool would ship uninsured wool
when the rivers were at flood depth and had
effaced the map. And with the sophistry that comes
so readily to help a man in his silliness, and which
is the only ally the gods then give him, Oane
ordered his engineer to “ stroke her up,”and “let
her rip.”
And, five days from Menindie, the Saucy Nell
did, and her barge with her, in the very next
moment. They were, so Cane suspected, some-
where about Tapio, in the main channel. But he
saw nothing of the homestead, only the whitened
carcases of drowned stock with hawks rejoicing
over the Apician banquets. The irony of it!
The birds were feeding, not on carrion, but on
gold !
A deck-hand was at the wheel. Oane was
for’ard, reckless and moody, his spirits tempered
by the gloomy day. They had given up the mono-
tonous poling. “Let her rip—and be damned!”
Then The boat scraped—shivered—throbbed !
The paddles jarred but did not stop. A groaning
and rending followed as she drove onwards—and
then a gurgling as the water forced its way
between the knees.
Cane was alert and sobered now. The immi-
nence of danger re-spiced bis energies. When it
was too late he shouted out his orders. The
seething water would not, however, let the words
reach the ears of the engineer. “Full astern!”
might have saved her from possibly more damage.
But, with “Full ahead !” on the dial, more rend-
ing of planks, more gashing of cross-timbers—even
a stanchion supporting the machinery decking
was torn from its sockets by the huge, protuberant
mg that had worked the mischief. When the
stanchion had gone the engineer understood
and shut off steam 4 Then the great lo
held her; and as, in the murkiness of the
day and the brooding monotony of the trip,
the bargeman, too, had dulled to his duty he
let the barge come unchecked right stem on to the
steamer. The shock of the contact made her
recoil, but leading by its violence to the displace-
ment of the top tiers of wool, these broke their
lashings, and by a sudden shifting forward drove
the barge once more upon the steamer, which was
now settling down in about fifteen feet of water.
The second impact and the movement of the cargo
served the barge a like fatal turn to that which
had ruined the Saucy Nell. She quivered from
stem to stern ; the wheel-platform, thrown out of
gear by the displacement of the wool, rendered
the rudder-chains useless; she passed from a trim,
tautly-held craft into an unmanageable mass. A
plank started forward, and the surging in of the
stream did the rest. She, too, settled down.
The men made their way as best they could to
the pilot-house. That was the only unsubmerged
part of the craft. They cursed their luck and
their skipper. And Cane listened without a word,
for he knew that he deserved all their impreca-
tions. The devil was in him that he had lost his
head ! With the heavens bursting above him and
the new waters coming down to swell the flood
around him, he and his boats might be doomed to
stop there a month—perhaps two, perhaps three.
A.nd where was he in the stream —within hauling
distance of it, or half a mile inwards ?”
The men gibed him, and he stood their rudeness
patiently. But when one more thoughtful than
others said, “This will about fix up Fairfax, of
Caloonaga !” Cane asked him his meaning.
“Fairfax’s storekeeper told me as he was
a-tallyin’ out the clip from the shed that this was
Fairfax’s last show.”
“How d’ye mean ?”
“ Why, he meant as the bank is a-comin’ down
on the cove, unless he’d got this wool to ’Chuca
within a for’night ! They gave him that chance—
he was to deliver his wool at the w’arves to their
agent, or be clewed up and cleared out. This was
his last show—an’ now you’ve damned it nicely !’’
“ An’ Fairfax,” broke in the mate, “ told me as
his open policy had run out, so he ain’t insured 1”
Cane, rough enough, but not a man to bluff and
bounce in the presence of calamity for which he
was responsible, said nothing. What was the use
of saying anything when nothing could be done?
And there was nothing to be done. Snagging in
a low river is not the greatest of disasters, bad
though it be. Snagging away from the river-
banks, in flood-time, is a very different thing.
Only a fellow who can draw drafts upon a special
Providence with a certainty of their being
honored, could laugh at it.
No, there was nothing to be done except to wait
for the waters to go down ; except to send out the
dingy in search of a station homestead where the
men might obtain tucker and shelter.
• * ft
And Fairfax?
Not always do ill tidings fly. Circumstance
makes Time a laggard, sometimes, even when his
wallet is burdened with woe instead of weal.
It was three weeks before “the cove” at
Caloonaga heard the news, though he was but
a day’s coaching from the spot where the
snagged crafts rested. Eight miles from Tapio
homestead, and a good quarter of a mile from the
river banks, they lay. That was what the mate
in charge of the dingy had discovered. Tapio
had sent the intelligence to Wentworth by mys-
terious cross-country routes, which somehow
never passed within cooey of Caloonaga. And so
it was that when Fairfax heard of it the news
came from Echuca by a hawker’s launch, which
brought over also the mailman from Wentworth
side. The dreaded notice of foreclosure, and the
order to give delivery of everything to the Moama
Company’s agent, were in his loose-bag.
‘‘The floods being up may prevent Mr. ’s
arrival for some days after this should reach you,”
wrote the managing director, “ but at the earliest
possible moment he shall be with you, and relieve
you of your responsibility to us. . . . My
directors regret to be compelled to take the ex-
treme step of foreclosing, but their duty to our
shareholders leaves them no alternative, as you
did not take advantage of the time allowed for the
delivery of the balance of your clip to our order at
Echuca.”
So, with many flourishes of pen, professing him-
self, too, to be his, Fairfax’s, “ very faithfully,”
the managing director concluded, by one and the
same action, the letter and Fairfax’s career.
# * *
When the agent of the company arrived
to take charge, he took delivery of something not
in the bond —a suicide’s body.
It was weak and cowardly, of course. But
stronger men than Fairfax have refused the
contest with Fate when she encases herself in
sheets of parchment. The weapon of a courageous
spirit may cleave a coat-of-mail, but seldom a
mortgage deed. And, in this case, Finance and
Law were aided by sardonic Nature.
Nature has no pity for the weak. Neither has
the Law ; nor Finance.
PRICH WARUNG.
Andy Payne is a noted teamster—a sort of
slow-time_ Yuba Bill—up Wilcannia way, and is
more particular about whom he drinks with than
many a Governor. One day a little neurotic
storekeeper, living in that district, and mostly
in a state of jimjams, who entertained for Andy
the kind of admiration which weak, chattering
little men usually have for strong, self-contained
ones, met Andy in a pub. “ Join me in er drink,
ole man,” he said familiarly, tapping the teamster
on the arm. Andy glowered, but made no reply.
“ Whas matter, Andy?” said the other, “you
know me, don’t you?” “Know you,” said Andy,
with infinite scorn. “The last time I saw you,
you was fightin’ with a gohanna in a tin hut.
Gerrout! ”
Sydney Olivier, son-in-law to an English
judge and one of the authors of the famous
Fabian essays on Socialism, thus rushes into the
breach on the breeches controversy in London
Daily Chronicle
The characteristic formation of hips and legs that
makes most adult women look ridiculous in knickerbooker
costume has been imposed on the normal woman as the
condition of maternal survival. On this ground it seems
likely to persist; and the skirt to persist in its company
from considerations of convenience as well as beauty.
But we all of us know women who look, or would look,
quite as graceful in knickerbockers as they do in skirts, if
not more so, and why any ungrown girl at all should wear
skirts, I cannot imagine. We may be sure that the un-
gracefully-shaped women are not likely, as a rule, to take
to breeches if they value their appearance above con-
venience. If they don’t, what will men’s ridicule or re-
monstrances avail, which again and again have proved
powerless against inelegance and inconvenience of fashion
combined ? The knickerbockerophobist may take comfort
if he reflects that it cannot be convenient to most women
or compatible with the vanity of many, to wear knicker
bookers continually all their lives.
“I am confident,” remarked Queen ViC-
toria to the British Parliament, “ that you will
share in the joy with which I and my people have
welcomed the birth of an heir in the third genera-
tion to my Throne, an event not merely pro-
pitious, but unprecedented in the history of this
country.” Of England, yes. But not unpre
cedented in history. Louis XIV. of France once
made a similar boast. He and his eldest son, and
his eldest son’s eldest son, and his eldest ’son’s
eldest son’s eldest son were all alive at the same
time. But he lived to see them all dead. What’s
the betting even now that Princess May, of the
Dead Heart, will ever be Queen of England ?

( Vl< ~) >n <<To the Australian natives who now act
in the Turner Ministry, we would say : ‘Do not
forget your progenitors!’ but, as they fought
manfully, and against mighty odds, for the open-
mg-up of these wondrous and fertile lands in the
past, do ye likewise in the governing and direction
now, so that when your term of office comes to an
honorable end— as your chief has already promised
and predicted it will do—the hearts of Queen YiC-
toria’s subjects in this colony may cry out to you
in gratitude for the good you have done, and the
true nobility with which you have done it. Let
no false sentiment of nationality and nativity
crush out the love and reverence due to the hoary-
headed and toil-worn pioneers of this colony and
the Australian Continent, in the hearts of the
younger generation born of the soil. Ah ! No.
Give all honor to the brave-hearted and honest
forefathers who opened-up a new country, which
under the blessing of God, will yet become the
seat of all that is good, and true, and noble in
learning, education and refinement through-
out the dominions of Victoria, our Gracious
Queen and Empress.” This get-down-on-your-
marrows-and-worship-your-grandfather balder-
dash tires me. What have the old pioneers
done for me that I should be called upon
to whoop every time they are mentioned ?
What have “ these brave-hearted and honest fore-
fathers who opened up my new country ” done to
deserve even my respectful homage? Nixey— nil
nothing ! They’ve absolutely deserved my
execration. The total proceeds squandered by
them foot up £1,082,000,000, made up 50£200,000,000
£2o0,000,000 gold, .£182,000,000 Government ex-
penditure, £700,000,000 of products, £50,000 000
of public debts, and £150,000,000 private loans.
All this good “stuff” has been “done in” in 40
years by a mere handful of bunglers who sold the
finest land in the world to land-thieves at £1 an
acre, who saddled us with a code of commercial
laws which would disgrace Greece or Turkey, and
who have left the young generation of Victorians
to work like galley-slaves at the oar to pay RENT
and INTEREST to absentee land-owners and
money-lenders. It’s time the old pioneer climbed
down.—Yours, &c., Damkot, Vic.
